# Chapter 1

## **Chapter 1**

Roses, the troubadour sang, and the words were wrong. Guillaume’s fingers tightened on the lute’s neck. Not *roses*, but *la rosa*—the precise, sun-warmed thorn of it from a verse he’d copied twenty years prior. The memory was a physical knot behind his eyes, frayed and true.



It lay on the oak lectern, pale and accusing in the thin light from the high window. The ink, my own mixture of oak gall and iron salts, was a dried black river delta of failed phrases. I could smell its sharp, metallic tang, mixed with the scent of beeswax from the smoothing stone and the older, deeper smell of the vellum itself—sheepskin, lime, a ghost of meadow grass. I’d scraped it clean three times. The surface was growing tired, a little fuzzy, a little too thirsty. One more failure and the skin would be ruined, fit only for kindling or for wrapping fish.

The line I was murdering was simple. *De moi doleros vos chant.* “Of my sorrow, I sing to you.” The opening of a *canso* by Bernart de Ventadorn, dead a hundred years. My task was not to copy it. Any monk in the scriptorium could do that, his mind wandering to supper. My task was to *remember* it. To hear it in the langue d’oc of the Midi, to feel the shape of its melody against my ribs, to see the face of the lady to whom it was first sung—a face I had never seen, in a court I would never visit, in a century that was dust. And then, to write it down *as if I were Bernart*, the ink flowing from the same well of sorrow.

I was failing because I could remember too much.

The memory wasn’t a single thread. It was a tapestry, frayed and heavy. When I reached for the tune, I got the scent of hot stone and rosemary from the courtyard where my master, Arnaut, first hummed it for me. I saw the chipped blue of his tankard. I felt the particular ache in my lower back from sitting on the hard stool that day. The words “*De moi doleros*” brought not the idealized, cruel lady of the song, but the face of Alais, the baker’s daughter in our village, with a smudge of flour on her nose and a laugh that cracked like a whip. My own, stupid, un-poetic sorrows clambered over the ancient, refined ones, muddying them.

“You are thinking of your own bellyache,” a voice said from the doorway. “Or your own lust. It is a common error.”

Magister Humbert did not enter so much as occupy the threshold. He was a long, dry man, his Benedictine habit hanging from his frame like a sack from a dead branch. His face was all severe angles, the skin taut over his cheekbones, but his eyes were the startling blue of a winter sky, and they missed nothing.

“I am trying to isolate the memory, Magister.”

“Isolate.” He tasted the word, found it sour. “You are a rememberer, Guilhem, not a surgeon. You do not dissect. You *invite*. You let the memory inhabit you. The sorrow, the joy, the spring breeze, the lady’s glance. It is a guest. You must provide a clean house.”

“My house is crowded,” I muttered, looking back at the spoiled vellum.

“Then you must learn to be a better host. To clear space for the nobler visitor.” He glided into the small, cold chamber I’d been given in the abbey’s west range. His fingers, pale and precise, touched the edge of the parchment. “Bernart’s sorrow is a fine wine. You are trying to taste it while chewing on yesterday’s greasy mutton. No wonder it tastes of nothing.”

He was right. That was the hell of it. My gift—my curse—was total recall. I remembered everything. The exact pattern of frost on a leaf on a Tuesday morning when I was seven. Every word of a drunken argument between my parents. The taste of every meal, the feel of every wool tunic against my skin, the sound of every snore from every man in a dormitory. It was all in here, a roaring, jostling crowd. Asking one memory to step forward, alone and pristine, was like asking a single drop of water to leap from a raging river.

“Perhaps I am not meant for this,” I said, the words ash in my mouth. I had said them before. They never changed anything.

“Perhaps you are not,” Humbert agreed, which was worse. “The Lord gives gifts for His purposes, not ours. Your memory is a perfect storehouse. But a storehouse is useless if the steward cannot find the one barrel of vintage he needs. He must know every cask, every label, and have the discipline to ignore all but the one.” He fixed me with his winter-blue eyes. “You lack discipline. You lack the architecture of forgetting.”

“How does one architect forgetting?”

“You begin,” he said, turning to leave, “by finishing. Clean your vellum. Still your mind. Listen for the song, not the singer. The lady, not the baker’s daughter. I will return at Vespers. Have the first stanza. Not a copy. A *recollection*.”

He was gone, leaving the scent of faint incense and mild disappointment.

I closed my eyes. I pushed away the smell of the ink, the ache in my neck, the grumble of my stomach. I pushed away the image of Alais’s floury smile. I reached back, through the clutter, to Arnaut.

He had been old, my master, his voice a rusted hinge of a thing, but his ear was perfect. We were in his workshop, surrounded by the tools of his trade as a cooper—the adzes, the drawknives, the steaming trough for bending staves. The smell was of fresh-cut oak and sweat. He was fitting hoops onto a barrel, the *tap-tap-tap* of his hammer a steady rhythm.

“Listen, boy,” he’d said, not looking up. “This one, it’s like a key turning in a lock. You hear it, you understand the whole room.”

And he began to sing, his old voice finding a surprising, fragile sweetness on the high notes.

*De moi doleros vos chant,*
*car sai que del miei dam*
*no·us cal, si be·us sui amaran...*

Of my sorrow I sing to you,
For I know that of my pain
You care not, though I am loving you…

The *tap-tap-tap* of his hammer became the heartbeat of the song. The shavings of oak at his feet were the fallen leaves of the lyric’s autumn. I saw it then, not as words on skin, but as a thing alive—a man’s heart, polished and presented, honed by form into something both beautiful and devastating. Arnaut’s rough hands, shaping wood, shaping song. The memory was whole. The guest had arrived.

I opened my eyes. The clutter in my mind had receded. The clean, sharp sorrow of Bernart was there, a cold, bright crystal. I took up my fresh quill, honed the nib with my knife. I did not think of letters. I thought of the melody. I let it guide my hand.

The ink flowed this time, not as my script, but as the script of the memory. The graceful, angular Occitan letters formed themselves. I heard the rhyme, felt the internal music of the *canso’s* structure, and my hand obeyed. It was not perfect—my ‘s’ was a little shaky—but it was true. It was a recollection.

When Humbert returned as the bells for Vespers began to toll, he stood behind me and read the six lines I had written. He was silent for a long time. I could hear the brothers shuffling along the cloister outside, their sandals whispering on stone.

“Better,” he said finally. There was no praise in it. It was an assessment. “You have made room. But you have only hosted one guest. A *canso* has many stanzas. A life has many songs. The discipline is in maintaining the emptiness between them. The architecture is in the walls you build to keep your own life from crashing the feast.”

“What if my own life is the feast?” I asked, suddenly weary. “What if all these memories… are the point?”

He gave me a look that was almost pity. “Then you are not a rememberer for this abbey, Guilhem. You are merely a man with a crowded head. Our work here is to preserve what is *other*. What is beautiful and dying. The songs of the troubadours, the debates of the scholars, the voices of a world the Church now views with… suspicion. We save them from the fire of ignorance and the slow rot of time. We do not save Guilhem of Mirepoix. He is irrelevant.”

He placed a new, pristine sheet of vellum on the lectern. “Tomorrow, the second stanza. And you will begin the architectural exercises. After Prime, you will report to the north garden. Brother Anselm will instruct you.”

“Instruction in what?”

“In forgetting,” Humbert said, and left me with the fading light and the perfect, sorrowful lines that were not mine.

---

That night, in the narrow cell that was my own, I tried to sleep. But sleep for me was never an escape; it was merely another gallery in the museum of my mind. Memories rose unbidden.

I was nine years old, and my father was teaching me to mend a fishing net. His hands, thick and rope-scarred, moved with a swift, sure magic. Mine were clumsy, all thumbs. “The trick,” he said, his voice a low rumble I felt in my chest as I leaned against him, “is to see the hole not as a break, but as a shape to be filled. You don’t fight the emptiness. You compliment it.” The smell of tar and river-water was so strong I could taste it.

I was fifteen, drunk on cheap wine behind the tavern, and Alais was letting me kiss her. Her mouth tasted of leeks and ale, and she giggled into my lips. It was thrilling and profoundly awkward, and I remembered the precise pressure of her hands on my shoulders, pushing me away after a moment, still laughing. “Enough, poet,” she’d said. I’d written my first terrible verse for her that night, now etched in my mind with permanent, cringing shame.

Memory upon memory. A cacophony of selves. Humbert wanted me to build walls, to create a silent, empty chamber where only the sanctioned, beautiful ghosts of the past could enter. But my life was in the noise. My father’s tar-stained wisdom was in the net-mending. My own clumsy desire was in that kiss. Was that not also worth keeping?

But then I saw the vellum, the perfect black lines of Bernart’s sorrow. It was a different kind of truth—not lived, but crafted. Not experienced, but *sung*. It had a purity my messy life could never approach. Perhaps that was the point. Perhaps we needed both: the messy life to live, and the perfect song to make sense of it. My role, it seemed, was to be the bridge. The rememberer. The one who could hold the perfect song intact, even if he could never live it.

The bell for Matins echoed through the cold stones. I had not slept. I rose, my body aching, and splashed water on my face from the basin. In the dark, I dressed. I was not a monk, but I lived by their rhythms. As I walked the silent cloister towards the church, the stars were a hard, bright dust above the open garth. The memory of the sky from last night, and the night before, and a thousand nights before that, pressed in on me. I focused on my footsteps. One. Then another. Creating, step by step, a tiny, present emptiness.

---

After the sparse, silent breakfast of bread and ale, I found Brother Anselm in the north garden. It was a utilitarian space: herb beds, a few fruit trees, a compost heap steaming gently in the morning chill. Anselm was on his knees, weeding a patch of sage. He was a compact man, built like a badger, with forearms thick from digging and a face perpetually smudged with earth.

“You are Guilhem,” he said, not looking up. His voice was a pleasant gravel. “The one who can’t forget.”

“So I am told.”

“Magister Humbert says you are to learn architecture from me.” He sat back on his heels and looked at me. His eyes were a warm brown, surrounded by a web of laughter lines. “Do I look like an architect to you?”

“No, Brother.”

“Good. I’m a gardener. And gardening is the best training for the mind there is.” He pointed a dirty finger at the row of sage. “See this? Every weed you pull is a stray thought. A personal memory. An itch, a regret, a hunger. You must remove it, cleanly, by the root. But you must not disturb the sage—the memory you are meant to keep. The task requires attention, gentleness, and ruthlessness.”

He handed me a small trowel and pointed to a nearby bed of thyme, infested with chickweed. “Begin. And as you work, you will recite to me the *Alba* of Giraut de Bornelh. The one that begins ‘*Reis glorios*’.”

I stared at him. “I am to recite a dawn song while weeding?”

“You are to hold the song, perfect and entire, in the front of your mind. The weeding is the test. If your mind wanders to your backache, or to the girl you left behind, or to what’s for dinner, you will nick the thyme. You will leave a root. The song will falter. The architecture is in the division: the song is the cultivated plant. Everything else is weed. Now. Begin.”

I knelt on the damp earth. The cold seeped through my hose. I found the first chickweed, a sprawling, tenacious thing. I inserted the trowel, careful of the thyme’s low, woody stems. I began to recite, my voice shaky in the morning air.

“*Reis glorios, verais lums e clartatz…*” Glorious king, true light and brightness…

As I spoke the words, the memory surfaced: not of a troubadour, but of Humbert’s voice, drilling me on the pronunciation. I saw the page. I felt the frustration. That was a weed. I pushed it aside, seeking the pure song. I found its melody, a plaintive climb towards dawn.

My fingers worked. A weed came free, its roots trailing damp soil. The thyme trembled, unharmed. I moved to the next.

“*Deus poderos, Senher, si a vos platz…*” Mighty God, Lord, if it pleases you…

A memory of my mother praying, her hands clasped so tight her knuckles were white. A weed. I extracted it.

The work was agonizingly slow. My mind was a field of chickweed. Every line of the song triggered a personal association, a sensory ghost. The “watchman on the tower” made me think of the look-out in Mirepoix, old Peyre, who used to throw apples at us. A weed. I pulled it. The “faithful friend” brought Arnaut’s face, smiling. Was that a weed? The sage, or the chickweed? I hesitated. My trowel slipped. I severed a thin runner of thyme.

“Ah,” Anselm said softly from behind me. “You see? You must choose. Is your master part of the song? Or is he a distraction from it? For this work, he is a distraction. You must love the song more than the man who taught it to you.”

It felt like a betrayal. But I nodded. I went back, reciting from the beginning. *Reis glorios…* This time, I built a wall. Arnaut was outside it. My mother was outside it. The cold in my knees was outside it. Only the watchman, the longing for dawn, the plea to God remained inside, crystalline.

I weeded for two hours. My back screamed. My hands grew numb. But a small, clean patch emerged around the thyme. And the *alba* lived in my mouth, unsullied, its structure holding my mind upright.

When the bell rang for Terce, Anselm told me to stop. He looked at my small, cleared patch, at the pile of wilted chickweed.

“A start,” he said. “The garden of your mind is vast and overgrown. You will spend many hours here. You will learn that forgetting is not a loss. It is a making of space. It is the most charitable thing you can do for a beautiful memory—to give it a room of its own, without the clutter of your own small life.”

I stood, my joints protesting. The recited song faded, and my own memories came flooding back—the ache, the cold, the image of Anselm’s dirty hands. But for those two hours, there had been a kind of order. A fragile, weeded architecture.

As I walked back to the scriptorium, the remembered scent of sage clinging to my fingers, I understood the first, brutal lesson: to be the rememberer for this abbey, I would have to become a stranger to myself. I would have to learn to love the perfect, borrowed sorrow of a dead man more than the imperfect, living ache of my own heart.

The parchment awaited. The second stanza of Bernart’s *canso* demanded its empty room. I sat down, flexed my stiff fingers, and reached for the memory of a sorrow that was not mine, building the walls, brick by mental brick, against the rising tide of my own life.

# Chapter 2

## **Chapter 2**

The stone of the abbey was not silent. It hummed.

Brother Maurice, the ancient librarian who looked like a bundle of sticks wrapped in parchment, told me that on my third day. We were in the *armarium*, the small, secure room off the main library where the most precious items were kept. The air was thick with the smell of aged leather, dust, and the faint, sweet decay of organic matter slowly surrendering to time.

“It is the memory of the rain,” Maurice whispered, his voice the sound of pages turning. He ran a translucent, veined hand along a shelf of massive antiphonaries. “A hundred years of rain, absorbed. The memory of the masons’ chisels. The prayers of the brothers, soaked into the mortar. It is all here, humming. You, of all people, should hear it.”

I listened. At first, there was only the drip of a leak somewhere in the gutter outside, the distant chant from the church. Then, beneath it, I felt it—a low, sub-audible thrum, a vibration in the teeth more than a sound. It was the weight of centuries, compressed into limestone and intent. My own crowded head echoed in sympathy.

“You are to begin the Catalog,” Magister Humbert said, entering the *armarium* with a scroll case of worn leather under his arm. He placed it on the heavy oak table as if it contained the bones of a saint. “This is the foundation of your architectural training. You cannot order the memories of the world until you can order the objects that carry their echo.”

The Catalog was legendary among the few of us who knew of the abbey’s true work. It was not an inventory. It was a taxonomy of memory. A record of every song, poem, letter, and fragment in the abbey’s collection, cross-referenced by author, theme, melodic mode, historical context, and—most importantly—by the quality of memory required to access it.

Humbert unrolled the scroll. It was a thing of beauty and terrifying complexity. Columns of text in Latin, Occitan, Old French, even Arabic. Symbols I did not recognize: a lute with three strings, a tower with a missing stone, a rose half-eaten by moths.

“This,” Humbert said, pointing to the rose symbol next to an entry for a *tenso* by the Countess of Dia, “denotes a memory that is particularly… fragrant. Potent. It requires a rememberer with strong associative recall—one for whom scent is a primary trigger.” He looked at me. “You, for instance, seem burdened by smells.”

“And this?” I pointed to the tower with the missing stone.

“Fragmented. Incomplete. The memory is damaged, either in the source or in its transmission. Reconstructing it requires inferential skill—building the missing stone from the shape of the gap.” He rolled the scroll further. “Your task, starting with this cabinet, is to handle each item. Not to read it fully. To *taste* it. To feel what kind of memory it holds. Then, you will assign it symbols. You will begin to build the architecture in practice.”

He left me with Brother Maurice, who blinked his milky eyes and gestured to a locked cabinet of dark walnut. “The *fin’amor* cabinet. Courtly love. The most dangerous and beautiful of our collections. Begin with the lower left. And for God’s sake, wash your hands.”

The ritual was meticulous. A basin of clean water and a linen towel stood on a side table. I washed, drying my hands until they were chapped. Maurice produced a large iron key and unlocked the cabinet. The door swung open with a sigh.

Inside were not books, but sheaves. Folded letters. Single pieces of vellum, some no larger than my palm, tucked into linen sleeves. A few more substantial codices, their bindings cracked. Each was tagged with a slip of parchment noting its provenance. *Copied from the recitation of Jaufré Rudel, Acre, 1148. Found in the effects of a jongleur, Marseille, 1211. Sung at the court of Eleanor, 1152.*

My first item was a single sheet, its edges nibbled by time or mice. The linen sleeve was stained with something dark—wine, perhaps, or blood. The tag read: *Unverified. Lament for a Lost Falcon. Possibly Languedoc, late 12th century.*

I lifted it out, my heart thudding strangely. This was not a clean copy made in the scriptorium. This was the thing itself. A artifact of the moment. I laid it on the table, using lead weights to hold the corners.

The script was hurried, a personal hand, not a scribe’s. The Occitan was simple, the metaphors rustic. It was indeed a lament for a lost falcon, but the bird was so lavishly described—its feathers “the grey of a Lenten sky,” its eyes “two polished jet beads holding the hunter’s pride”—that the subtext screamed. This was no falcon. This was a person. A lover, lost.

I did not just read it. I did as Humbert instructed. I closed my eyes and placed my fingertips lightly on the cool vellum, just beside the ink. I let the object speak.

A memory, not my own, rose.

Not a full scene. A sensory shard. The smell of rain on dry earth. The feel of a leather gauntlet, stiff with salt from a sweating palm. A gust of wind, carrying the shriek of a distant kite. And beneath it, a raw, gut-punch of grief, so immediate it stole my breath. It was not the refined, artistic sorrow of Bernart. This was ugly, choking, *fresh*.

I snatched my hand back, gasping. The vellum lay inert. Brother Maurice watched me, unblinking.

“Strong?” he asked.

I nodded, unable to speak. The taste of that grief was in my mouth, metallic.

“That is the echo,” he said. “The memory of the one who wrote it, trapped in the act of writing. The ink is not just pigment. It is a fossil of feeling.” He handed me a fresh slip of parchment and a fine-nibbed pen. “Now. Classify it.”

My hand shook. I dipped the pen. What symbols? The grief was not fragrant. It was acrid, like smoke. It was not fragmented; it was a single, brutal blow. I drew a simple, quick mark: a downward stroke, like a falling bird. Then I added another symbol from Humbert’s scroll: a tear, but I made it jagged, not round.

Maurice peered at it. “A good start. The falling stroke for the sudden loss. The jagged tear for the pain that is not poetic, but tearing. You are not cataloging the text. You are cataloging the ghost in it. Proceed.”

Item by item, the morning unspooled. A cheerful, bawdy *sirventes* by a jongleur left a ghost of smoky tavern laughter and the itch of flea bites. A philosophical *tenso* between two scholars carried the dry, pleasant ghost of dust and wax tablets, the feeling of a logical puzzle clicking into place. A woman’s song, copied in a graceful, educated hand, held the complex ghost of constraint—the scent of lavender sachets, the feel of tight brocade, a longing that was a quiet, constant hum, like the stone of the abbey itself.

I was building a map. Not of knowledge, but of human feeling, fossilized in words. Each symbol I drew was a cornerstone in this invisible architecture. This wing for sharp grief. This corridor for muted longing. This vault for intellectual joy.

It was exhausting in a way copying never was. Each ghost brushed against my own memories, seeking resonance. The tavern laughter found my own memories of Arnaut’s rough jokes. The scholar’s puzzle-touch found the satisfaction of fitting a net-mend. The constrained longing found… Alais. Always Alais. I had to weed constantly, as Anselm had taught me. To acknowledge the personal echo, then wall it off. *That is your memory. This is the artifact’s. Keep them separate.*

By Nones, my head was a roaring cathedral of other people’s ghosts. I needed air.

Humbert found me in the cloister, leaning against a column, my face turned to the weak sun.

“The Catalog is a mirror,” he said, joining me. “It shows you the architecture of your own mind. What clings to you? What do you instinctively resonate with? The falconer’s grief gripped you. The scholar’s debate, less so. This tells you something.”

“It tells me I am a peasant with simple emotions,” I said, bitterness welling up.

“It tells you your memory is visceral. Sensory. Earthy. This is not a weakness. It is your particular strength. You will be our best rememberer for the songs of the body. For the laments of loss that are felt in the gut, not the head. But you must learn to channel it. To let the ghost flow through you without getting stuck in your own mud.”

He handed me a small, hard object wrapped in soft leather. “A test. A different kind of artifact. Catalog it tonight, in your cell. Use no light but a single candle.”

---

In the flickering cave of light my single candle made, I unwrapped the leather. It was a wax tablet. Two wooden leaves, hollowed out and filled with smooth, dark beeswax, hinged together with leather cords. The writing surface was covered with scratches, words and musical notations stylized into near-abstraction by haste. This was not a fair copy. This was a workshop. A composer’s thoughts, caught in flight.

The tag, in Humbert’s precise hand, read: *Folquet de Marseille, draft fragment. Circa 1195. Theme: Crusade. Unfinished.*

Folquet. The troubadour who abandoned song for God, becoming first a Cistercian monk, then the Bishop of Toulouse, a fierce persecutor of the Cathars he once sang among. A man who turned his back on one love for another.

I touched the wax. It was cool, slightly greasy.

The ghost that rose was a tempest.

It was not a single feeling, but a war. A soaring melodic line of earthly desire—the smell of sun-warmed skin, the taste of spiced wine, the dizzying pull of a lover’s gaze—clashed against a crushing, ascetic counter-melody of guilt: the chill of stone floors, the sting of the hair shirt, the hollow echo of a plainsong chant. The words were half-formed, scratched out, re-scratched. *“Pois me laisset en cossirier…”* “Since she left me in torment…” Was “she” a woman? Or the world? Or God Himself?

The conflict was alive, unresolved. It was not a fossil. It was a trapped scream. The wax seemed to throb under my fingers with the man’s agony of choice.

My own memories surged, not as weeds this time, but as a sympathetic tide. My father’s simple faith, as solid as his net-mending. My mother’s quiet doubts, voiced only in sighs. My own confusion, torn between the vibrant, messy life of Mirepoix and this austere, beautiful prison of memory. I was no Folquet, but I knew the shape of the rift. The feeling of being pulled in two.

The ghost gripped me. I couldn’t wall it off. It was too big, too familiar. The candle guttered. I was no longer in my cell. I was in the storm of Folquet’s soul, feeling the exquisite torture of a man trying to carve his own heart in two with a stylus.

I must have made a sound. A groan, or a sob.

The door to my cell opened. Magister Humbert stood there, a silhouette against the torchlight in the corridor. He saw the open tablet, my face wet with tears that were not wholly my own.

He did not chastise me. He stepped inside, closed the door, and sat on the stool opposite my pallet. For a long moment, he just looked at me.

“You felt it,” he said. It was not a question.

“It is… tearing him apart.”

“It did tear him apart. He chose God. Or God chose him. The song died. The fragment is all that remains of the war.” Humbert leaned forward, his intense eyes capturing the candlelight. “This, Guilhem, is the peril and the purpose. You have a heart that resonates. That is why you are here. A colder man would feel nothing from that wax. He would see only scratches. You feel the civil war. That is a gift.”

“It feels like a curse. It hurts.”

“Good,” Humbert said, and there was a frightening fervor in his voice. “It *should* hurt. You are touching a live wound across time. Our work is not embalming. It is *resonance*. We keep these voices alive not as dead words, but as living feeling. To do that, the rememberer must feel them. But—” he held up a finger, “—you must not *drown* in them. You must become the lute, not the singer. The strings vibrate with the song, they feel it, they translate the touch into music. But when the song ends, the strings fall silent. They do not keep singing on their own.”

The lute, not the singer. The architecture, not the ghost.

“How?” My voice was a child’s whisper.

“By knowing yourself. By mapping your own resonances, as you did in the Catalog today. You now know the falconer’s grief hooks you deeply. You now know Folquet’s conflict mirrors your own. This knowledge is your blueprint. When you handle such items, you must build stronger walls beforehand. You must go in knowing you are entering a storm, and prepare your ship accordingly.”

He took the wax tablet from my trembling hands, wrapped it gently in its leather. “Tomorrow, you will not go to the *armarium*. You will return to Brother Anselm. You will weed the entire herb garden. You will recite the complete works of Peire Vidal from memory. You will ground yourself in the earth and in disciplined, structured recall. You will remind your body and your heart that you are here, now. That you are the lute.”

He stood to leave. At the door, he paused. “The architecture of forgetting is not about emptiness, Guilhem. It is about creating a dedicated space. You have felt a great ghost tonight. That is not failure. That is initiation. Now you must learn to build a room for it, so that you can visit, and then close the door.”

He left, taking the storm of Folquet with him.

I blew out the candle. In the perfect dark, the ghosts slowly receded. The hum of the stone returned. I was just a man, in a cell, on a mountain in the middle of France. My own memories—of a river, a laugh, a father’s hands—came back, familiar and small and mine.

I understood Chapter Two of my training: to be the bridge for these immortal ghosts, I had to know the precise weight and weakness of my own soul. The Catalog was not just for the artifacts. It was for me. I was the most fragile, important document in the collection. And I was just beginning to learn my own symbols.

# Chapter 3

## **Chapter 3**

Spring arrived not as a gentle guest, but as a muddy, riotous invasion. The mountain streams, swollen with meltwater, roared in the gorges below the abbey. In the garden, the weeds, empowered by rain and sun, waged a blitzkrieg against Brother Anselm’s ordered beds. My hands were permanently stained with earth, my nails rimmed black, my muscles singing a constant, dull hymn of ache.

The weeding had become my liturgy. Each morning after Prime, I knelt in the mud. Each morning, Anselm gave me a new text to hold. The complete *Cansos* of Arnaut Daniel, with their intricate, self-conscious rhymes (*leus chans*). The satirical *sirventes* of Bertran de Born, all political venom and martial drumbeats. I recited them, the words a protective incantation against the chaos of the garden and the chaos of my own mind. I was getting better. I could clear a bed of dandelions while holding the complex internal rhymes of a *tornada* perfectly in mind, my personal memories reduced to a distant murmur.

I was becoming an instrument.

Then the courier came.

He arrived just before Sext, a man built like a sack of grain on a tired horse. He was not a monk, nor a scholar. He wore the stained leathers of a professional messenger, a long dagger at his hip, and a look of profound relief at reaching his destination. He was escorted to the Abbot’s quarters, but the news, in a place as porous as an abbey, could not be contained.

Magister Humbert summoned me to the scriptorium. His face was a mask of calm, but his winter-blue eyes burned.

“Sit,” he commanded. I sat, my gardening trowel still in my belt, leaving a smudge of dirt on the bench.

“A message from the Chapter House in Toulouse,” he began, his voice low and urgent. “A library. A private collection, assembled by a minor lord with Cathar sympathies. He is dead. His heirs are… pragmatic. They fear possession of such texts will draw the attention of the Inquisition, which is currently making examples in the region.”

I knew of the Inquisition only as a distant thunderstorm, a rumor of fear and fire. It had not touched our remote mountain abbey directly.

“The heirs wish to dispose of the collection. Sell what is sellable, burn the rest. A canon in Toulouse, sympathetic to our work, has intervened. He has negotiated. For a sum, he can divert the collection here. But it must be done quickly, quietly, and he cannot sort it himself. It is four chests. A jumble. Heretical texts, troubadour songs, household accounts, medical treatises—all together. It is a midden heap of a man’s mind and his dangerous affiliations.”

He leaned forward. “You will go with Brother Maurice and two lay brothers. You will travel to the lord’s manor, three days’ ride from here. Your task is not to read. It is to *sense*. To use what you have learned in the Catalog. You will go through those chests, item by item. You will separate the wheat from the chaff, the precious from the perilous. What we want are the songs, the poems, the unique voices. What we must avoid are the blatantly heretical tracts—those are a fire that can burn us all. You will have one day. Then the chests with our chosen items return with us. The rest… the canon will see to it.”

My blood turned to ice water. Go out? Into the world? To a place of recent death and active fear?



---

# Chapter 4

## **Chapter 4**

The road was a tongue of dried mud, tasting of dust and dread.

It unspooled before our small party, a gray-brown ribbon laid over the shoulders of the hills. To our left, the land fell away into valleys choked with mist, white as spoiled milk. To our right, the mountains rose, their peaks sheathed in cloud, ancient and indifferent. I rode a placid mule named Patience, who smelled of hay and resignation. Behind me, the two lay brothers, Guillaume and Luc, guided the pack mules, their conversation a low, steady murmur about the best way to grease a cart axle. Ahead, Brother Maurice’s broad back was a wall of undyed wool, swaying with the gait of his horse. He had not spoken since we passed under the abbey gate at dawn, his silence a heavier burden than the satchel of bread and cheese strapped behind my saddle.

I was a bottle sealed too tight, my thoughts fermenting. The world outside the abbey walls was not a page to be turned, but a cacophony. It was the shriek of a jay in a pine copse, a sound like a rusted hinge. It was the raw, green smell of ferns crushed under hooves, not the muted scent of stone and vellum. It was the sheer *exposure* of it, the sky a vast, bowl of washed-out blue, with nowhere to hide. I felt my skin was parchment, stretched thin, and every sight, every sound, was a clumsy nib scratching upon it.

My hand went to my belt, not to the trowel, but to the small, hard shape of my smoothing stone, worn smooth by my own thumb. I carried it as another might a rosary. Its familiar weight was a knot to tie my mind to. *De moi doleros vos chant.* The line returned, not as poetry, but as a truth. Of my sorrow, I was a silent vessel, carrying it along this road.

We made camp at dusk in the lee of a granite outcrop. Guillaume and Luc were efficient, a well-practiced machine of gathering wood, striking flint, filling a small pot from a stream. A fire bloomed, its light a defiant, orange eye in the vast twilight. The smell of burning pine resin was sharp, medicinal.

Brother Maurice finally broke his fast and his silence with a piece of hard cheese. He looked at me across the flames, his face carved by the flickering shadows.

“You are quiet, Sebastian.”

“I am listening,” I said, which was not entirely a lie. I was listening to the emptiness inside me, the hollow where courage should have been.

“To the land?” he asked, his tone neutral.

“To its volume.”

He gave a slow nod, as if I had said something wise. “It shouts, does it not? After the quiet of the scriptorium. The world is a poor scribe. It makes its marks too boldly, with no care for spacing or grace.”

His understanding was a small warmth, separate from the fire. “Magister Humbert said I must *sense*,” I said, the words feeling clumsy. “Among the texts. How does one sense a heresy?”

Maurice chewed thoughtfully. He was a man of logistics, of supply ledgers and harvest yields, but he had been in the scriptorium long enough to absorb its metaphors. “A heresy is a text that speaks with a forked tongue. It uses the same words—Light, Love, Spirit—but twists their meaning. It is a palimpsest where the original, holy text has been scraped away and something… other… has been written in its place. You will feel it. It will not sit right on the soul. Like a stone in your shoe.”

“And if I miss one? If I let a stone through?”

“Then we are all lame,” he said simply. “But the Magister did not choose you for your piety. He chose you for your ear. You hear the breath between the words. That is what we need. Not a theologian. A listener.”

Guillaume tossed another branch on the fire. “They say the Inquisition in Toulouse,” he began, his voice a rough, comfortable gravel, “they use the *peine forte et dure*. Heavy stones. To persuade the silent to speak.”

The night seemed to press closer. Luc crossed himself.

“We are not going to Toulouse,” Maurice said, his voice a door closing on the subject. “We are going to a dead man’s house. Our business is with paper and ink, not with judges.”

But the words hung in the air, heavier than stones. I saw not paper, but kindling. I saw the black river delta of my own failed verses, and imagined it catching, the flames climbing the upstrokes, consuming the serifs, leaving only ash that smelled of nothing at all. The fire before me popped, sending up a shower of angry sparks.

* * *

The second day, the land began to change. The clean, sharp lines of our mountains softened, slumped into rolling hills. The pines gave way to scrub oak and thorny gorse. The villages we passed were not the tight, gray stone clusters of the high country, but poorer, meaner places. Houses were of daub and wattle, roofs sagging. Faces that watched us from fields were pinched, closed as fists. We saw no one on the road.

The air grew warmer, thick with the smell of damp earth and something else—a sweet, cloying rot, like overripe fruit. It was the smell of a land too low, without the clarifying cold of the heights.

My fear, a sharp, specific blade at first, had blunted into a dull, pervasive ache. It was in my shoulders, in the grind of the mule’s gait. I was a codex being jostled on a cart, my pages coming loose.

“We are in the *pays* of the *bons hommes* now,” Luc muttered, almost to himself. The Good Men. What others called Cathars. He said it the way one might say ‘wolves’.

Brother Maurice heard him. “Our task is the same. In, sort, out. We are gardeners come to clear a patch of blighted growth. We take the rare bloom, if any, and burn the rest. We do not debate the blight.”

But the metaphor curdled in my mind. I was no gardener here. In the abbey garden, I knew each plant—its name, its purpose, its season. Here, I was rootless, a spore drifting over unfamiliar ground.

That afternoon, we forded a river, the water brown and swift, chuckling coldly around the mules’ legs. On the far bank, the road worsened, becoming little more than a pair of ruts carved by recent cartwheels into the mud. The sky to the south was a bruised yellow.

“We’ll not make the manor tonight,” Maurice announced, squinting at the heavens. “There is a hospice of the Order of Saint John, a mile off the road. We seek shelter there.”

The hospice was a low, fortified grange. A sallow brother in a black habit with a white cross admitted us with a silent nod. The place had the feel of a held breath. In the common hall, a fire smoked in the hearth. A few other travelers huddled near it—a merchant with a bandaged foot, two dusty pilgrims bound for Compostela. We took bread and thin broth at a trestle table.

The merchant, eager for news, leaned toward Maurice. “You come from the north? From the mountains?”

“We do.”

“Wise. The south is… unsettled.” He dropped his voice. “They took the *seigneur* of the very manor you passed, the one by the three oaks. Not a week gone. For questioning.”

My spoon froze halfway to my mouth. Maurice’s face remained impassive. “Is that so?”

“Aye. His heirs are selling anything that isn’t nailed down. Liquidating, they call it. Before the Questioners come back and decide the whole estate is tainted.” The merchant’s eyes gleamed with grim gossip. “They say the old lord had a taste for books. Dangerous pastime.”

I felt the broth turn to clay in my stomach. We were not racing against pragmatic heirs. We were racing against the Inquisition’s shadow. We were scavengers picking over a corpse before the official mourners arrived.

Later, in the dank dormitory, on a straw pallet that smelled of mildew and strangers, I could not sleep. The merchant’s words echoed. *A taste for books.* I saw the four chests not as a midden heap, but as a tomb. A man’s mind, bricked up in wood and iron. And I was to be the grave-robber, feeling in the dark for jewels among the bones.

What was the difference, I wondered, between a heresy and a song? Both were written in ink. Both came from a place of longing. Bernart de Ventadorn sang of a love that was forbidden, that burned the soul. Was that so different from the Cathar’s love of a God too pure for this filthy world? Both were maps of a yearning for elsewhere. My task was to condemn one and save the other based on the shape of its letters, the music of its syllables. I was to be a judge of cadence.

The stone in my hand was slick with sweat. I had no piety to guide me. Only the Catalog’s cold taxonomy: *Secular, Vernacular, Troubadour, Anonymous*. And the new, unspoken category: *Fire.*

* * *

The third day dawned under a lid of low, fast-moving cloud. The sweet-rot smell was stronger. The land felt watchful.

We reached the manor by mid-morning. It was not a castle, but a fortified house of honey-colored stone, crouching in a shallow valley. No smoke rose from its chimneys. No dogs barked. The gate in the surrounding wall stood open, a slack mouth. In the courtyard, a handcart stood laden with a rolled tapestry and a pair of andirons, abandoned as if in mid-theft.

A nervous steward in a stained doublet met us. He did not give his name. His eyes skipped over us like water on a hot griddle.

“You are from the abbey? The canon said you would come. The items are in the library. You have until vespers. Then the canon’s men come for the rest.” He gestured to the cart. “We are… simplifying.”

He led us through a hall where the rushes were stale and a fly buzzed against a leaded window. The library was a surprise—a small, square room on the second floor, with a single window overlooking a neglected herb garden. It was not the soaring, sacred space of our scriptorium, but a rich man’s indulgence. And it had been ransacked.

Four iron-bound chests sat in the center of the room, lids thrown back. Around them, the floor was a snowdrift of discarded volumes and loose leaves. Books had been pulled from their shelves, gutted for their bindings’ gold leaf or precious gems, their pages tossed aside. A gorgeous psalter, its illuminated capital a riot of blue and gold, lay sprawled open, its spine cracked. It was a vision of intellect undone by greed and fear.

The steward saw my gaze. “My late master’s collection,” he said, a faint, defensive pride in his voice, quickly extinguished. “The heirs have taken what is obviously valuable. What remains is… the dregs. The canon said you might find something of interest to your order among them. Do what you must.” He fled, his footsteps hurried on the stairs.

For a moment, we stood in the silence of the violated room. The smell was here too—old paper, yes, and leather, but underneath, the ghost of that sweet decay, as if the ideas themselves were putrefying.

Brother Maurice took command. “Guillaume, Luc, stand watch at the courtyard and the stair. One hour shifts. Let no one up. Sebastian.” He turned to me. His winter-blue eyes, so like the Magister’s, held no flame now, only a clear, cold light. “This is your ground. We are your hands. Tell us what to do.”

The weight of it descended, a physical pressure on my shoulders. I walked to the first chest. It was a chaos. A treatise on falconry atop a sheaf of legal documents in Occitan. A battered copy of Boethius’s *Consolation of Philosophy* nestled against a slim, unbound quire of vellum, its script a tight, secretive hand.

I picked up the quire. My fingers knew the quality of the skin—good, but not the best. The ink was a brownish-black. I did not read the words. I let my eyes blur. I *felt* the page.

It was a song. I knew it instantly. The lines were short, the phrases lyrical even in their silent form. The hand was hurried, a scribe capturing a voice from the air. *Quan vei l’aloete mover…* “When I see the lark moving…” My heart, a clenched thing, beat once, hard. It was a *canso*. A different voice from Bernart’s, but from the same world. A world of secular joy and sorrow. I set it carefully on the floor to my right. *Wheat.*

Next, a thick volume bound in worm-eaten boards. I opened it. A theological argument in Latin. The hand was beautiful, disciplined. But the content… it spoke of two principles, two gods, one of light, one of darkness. It spoke of this world as a prison of matter. The words were logical, serene, and utterly venomous. The stone in my soul turned. *Chaff.* I placed it to my left.

And so it began. The rhythm of it took me. The room, the world, fell away. There was only the stream of pages through my hands. My senses, overwhelmed for days, sharpened to a single, piercing point. I was not reading; I was divining. A medical text with Arabic diagrams—save. A household ledger of grain yields—burn. A fragment of a romance, the *Roman de la Rose*—save. A polemic against the Church of Rome, written in the vernacular, its rhetoric hot and jagged—burn.

Brother Maurice worked silently beside me, following my gestures, creating two piles that grew like strange, uneven towers. The wheat. The chaff. My back ached. My eyes burned. The dust of centuries and of recent violence filled my nostrils.

In the third chest, buried beneath a heavy Latin Bible, I found it.

It was not a book. It was a bundle, wrapped in oiled cloth and tied with a faded silk cord. It was small, the size of my palm. I unwrapped it. Inside was a single, folded sheet of the finest vellum I had ever touched. It was supple, almost soft, and of a breathtaking whiteness. Unscribed.

No. Not quite.

I held it to the light from the window. And there, visible only as a subtle change in the texture, a paleness within paleness, was writing. A ghost writing. A palimpsest. The original text had been scraped away with pumice, but the scribe had been too gentle, or the ink too tenacious. The memory of the letters remained, a watermark of meaning.

I could not read it. It was too faint, the script unfamiliar—not the clear Caroline minuscule of our abbey, nor the vernacular hand of the troubadours. It was spidery, angular. Greek, perhaps? Or something older?

But as I held it, a coldness seeped from the page into my fingertips. It was not the heresy of the dualist text, which argued and persuaded. This was different. This silence felt… hungry. It did not speak of a prison of matter, but of an absence at the heart of things. A void where God should be. It was the negative of faith.

My thumb found my smoothing stone. This was not wheat, nor chaff. This was something else. A seed of frost. A stone from a different shoe altogether.

“Sebastian?” Maurice’s voice was a distant thing.

I should have placed it on the left. With the fire-bound. But my hand refused. The Magister said to save the unique voices. This was the most unique voice I had ever encountered: a whisper from a scraped-clean slate. A memory of a text that refused to be forgotten.

I did not think. I acted. As Brother Maurice turned to lift another tome from the chest, I folded the oilcloth back over the faint, hungry page. I slipped the small bundle inside my tunic, against my skin. The cold of it was a shock, a brand.

The stone in my hand felt warm by comparison. I was no longer just a listener, a gardener, a judge.

I had become a thief. And the thing I had stolen was now sleeping against my heart, its silent, frost-bitten text waiting to be heard.



---

# Chapter 5

## **Chapter 5**

The cold was no longer a brand. It was a root. It had grown from the page, through my skin, and into the marrow of my ribs. A slow, crystalline tendril of silence, threading itself around the beat of my heart. I could feel it with every step away from the scriptorium, a second, colder rhythm counterpointing my own. The ghost text slept against me, a patient frost.

Brother Maurice’s chatter was the buzzing of a fly in a sealed room. “—the Magister will be pleased with the day’s culling, I think. A productive heresy is a contained heresy. Like draining an abscess.” He hefted the locked chest of condemned texts, his breath puffing in the chill of the cloister walk. The autumn air smelled of turned earth and decaying leaves, the monastery’s own version of scraping clean.

I nodded, my hand pressed absently to my chest, feeling the hard rectangle beneath the wool. “Yes. Contained.”

But it wasn’t. It was *here*. Moving with me. I had thought myself a vessel for voices, a gardener of words. Now I was soil for a seed I did not understand. The memory of my act—the swift, smooth theft—was a fresh, sharp script on my own soul. I could smell the metallic fear on myself, beneath the ink and wax.

“You are quiet, Sebastian.” Maurice paused, setting the chest down with a grunt. His small, bright eyes fixed on me. They were the eyes of a thrush, always seeing the worm, never the sky. “The work weighs on you. It is a holy weight. To hold the line against error is to feel its push.”

“It is the silence that weighs,” I said, and the truth of it surprised me. “The texts we burn… they are full of noise. Argument, passion, error. They *speak*. But the silence left behind… what does that become?”

Maurice blinked. “Peace. Orthodoxy. The clean vellum.”

I thought of the page against my skin, scraped clean but never empty. A vellum that remembered. “Yes,” I said. “The clean vellum.”

***

The chapter house was a throat of stone, waiting to swallow the evening. We stood in our appointed places, the brothers a field of brown wool and bowed heads. The Magister presided from his high seat, a figure carved from old wood and older doctrine. The day’s culling was to be sanctified, our violence given a liturgy.

But my mind was a trapped bird, beating against the cage of my ribs. The cold from the page had spread, a subtle numbness in my fingers. I kept them curled into my palms, hiding the tremor.

“We render thanks,” the Magister’s voice echoed, dry as a rattle, “for the clarity of our purpose. To separate the wheat from the chaff is divine agriculture. Yet remember: even the chaff has a use. It feeds the fire that bakes the bread. Thus, all error serves truth in the end.”

It was a tidy metaphor. I had always found comfort in its tidiness. Now, it felt like a child’s drawing of a storm. The thing sleeping against my heart was neither wheat nor chaff. It was the scythe.

The Magister’s gaze, slow and heavy as a rolling millstone, passed over the ranks. It settled on me. “Brother Sebastian. You worked with Brother Maurice today in the lower chests. Speak the tally.”

The air left my lungs. The cold in my chest sharpened, a needle of ice. All eyes were seeds pressed into my skin. I opened my mouth, and the lie I had prepared—a simple, adjusted number—dissolved on my tongue. The truth was a physical pressure behind my teeth, the ghost text demanding its own account.

“Seven dualist treatises, Magister,” I heard myself say, my voice a stranger’s in the hollow space. “One fragment of a Lollard sermon. And… one palimpsest. Of unknown origin.”

A ripple. A rustle of wool. The word *palimpsest* was a secular word, a scribe’s word. It spoke of reuse, of economy, of practical secrets. Not of heresy.

The Magister’s eyes did not waver. “Unknown origin? Describe it.”

“The substrate is vellum of exceptional quality. The overwriting is entirely scraped away. The under-text… remains as a ghost. A memory in the fiber. I could not read it.” Each word was a stone I laid between myself and the thing I had stolen. A fragile wall.

“And its disposition?”

This was the moment. The left hand, or the right. The fire, or the library. The cold against my heart was absolute. It was not persuasion. It was vacancy. To place it with the heretical texts felt like a different kind of lie. To name it unique, to save it… was to name what I had done.

“I…” The needle of ice became a splintering. “I deemed it… a unique voice. Worthy of… preservation. For study.” The silence that followed was thick enough to parch the tongue. I had broken the protocol. I had judged alone.

The Magister leaned forward, his knuckles white on the arms of his chair. “You deemed. You judged. The text itself gave you no argument? No content?”

“Its content is its absence, Magister.” The words fell from me, too revealing, too true. “It is a text that has been made to say nothing. But the nothing… has a texture.”

“Heresy is a positive force, Brother Sebastian,” he said, each word a chip of flint. “It asserts. It claims. It does not whisper. It shouts. A ghost is not a doctrine. Where is this page?”

The world narrowed to the point of his question. The cold in my chest was a well, and I was falling down it. “It is… in the scriptorium. On the preservation lectern.” The lie was ash in my mouth.

“Maurice.” The Magister did not look away from me. “Fetch it.”

Maurice scurried out, a blur of obedient brown. The wait was an excruciation. I counted the stones in the wall opposite. Thirty-seven. I counted the beats of the cold root in my chest. Each one a silent echo. I had become a palimpsest myself, the truth scraped away, the lie written fresh over the top. But the original sin, the ghost of the act, remained. I could feel it.

Maurice returned, his face pale, his hands empty. He hurried to the Magister, leaned close, and whispered. The old man’s face did not change. It was already stone. He listened, then lifted his hand for silence.

“The page is not on the preservation lectern,” he announced to the still air. “It is not in the scriptorium. Brother Maurice attests it was last in your care, Sebastian. Where is the unique voice?”

The accusation was not shouted. It was laid down like a shroud. Every brother became a statue. I was at the center of a frozen garden.

“I…” The word was a gasp. The cold had reached my throat. “I must have… misplaced it. In the confusion of the cull—”

“There is no confusion in duty,” the Magister interrupted. “Only failure. Or corruption.” He rose, a stark, tall shadow against the dying light of the high window. “You speak of the texture of nothing. You preserve a ghost. And now the physical fact is gone. This is not error, Sebastian. This is mimesis. You have become the text you describe. Absence where there should be presence. A void where there should be faith.”

He stepped down, his footsteps the only sound in the world. He stopped before me. I could smell the faint scent of lavender on his robes, the incense of sanctity over an old, dry core.

“The thief of a text steals more than parchment,” he said, his voice for me alone now, a blade so sharp I did not feel the cut. “He steals the words from the mouths of the faithful. He steals the light. You are a scribe. You know the penalty for a scribe who corrupts his sacred trust.”

I did. Excommunication. The stripping of the habit. The hands that held the pen could be severed, so they might never write error again. The tongue that spoke the lies could be removed, so it might never teach them. I saw it in his eyes: the clean, surgical logic of it. I was a corrupted page. I must be scraped clean.

The cold in my chest flared. It was not fear. It was something older. It was the hunger of the ghost text, recognizing its own. A void calling to a void.

“Search him,” the Magister said.

Hands grabbed me from behind—Brother Maurice, and the hulking form of Brother Augustin, the cellarer. They were not rough. They were efficient. They pinned my arms. Their touch was a violation of a different order, the community’s hands turning me into an object. The Magister’s own hands, thin and strong as talons, went to the neck of my tunic.

I did not struggle. I had gone empty. The thief was gone. Only the theft remained.

His fingers found the oilcloth bundle. He pulled it free. The air on my skin, where the page had lain, was a shock of raw heat. He unfolded the cloth on the open palm of his hand, holding the pristine, white, terrible vellum up for the chapter to see.

“Behold,” he said, his voice ringing now. “The unique voice.”

He carried it to the nearest window, the last grey-gold light of evening falling upon it. He squinted, bringing the page close to his aged eyes. He turned it. He tilted it. A frown etched deeper into his face.

He looked back at me, then again at the page. His certainty wavered, for a single, breathtaking instant.

“There is nothing here,” he said, and it was not a declaration of victory, but of confusion.

“The ghost writing,” I whispered, my voice cracking. “Hold it to the light. The palimpsest.”

He did. He held the page flat against the failing light. He stared. His breath slowed. The furious certainty drained from his posture, leaving something older, more brittle. He lowered the page and looked at me, and in his eyes I saw not anger, but a dawning, profound disquiet.

“Brother Maurice,” he said, his voice changed. “Come. Look. Tell me what you see.”

Maurice trotted over, took the page, performed the same ritual. His face, eager for confirmation, slowly puckered in bewilderment. “I… I see a fine vellum, Magister. Unscribed.”

“You see no trace? No memory of text? No… texture of nothing?”

“It is blank,” Maurice said, utterly confident in his deficiency.

The Magister took the page back. He stared at it as if it had bitten him. He looked at me, a man drowning in a metaphor he could not parse. I had confessed to preserving a ghost. I had been caught with the blank page. The evidence of my crime was its absence. The proof of my heresy was its invisibility.

I understood then. The ghost text did not show itself. It was not for them. It was for me. It had chosen its reader. In their hands, it was merely empty. In mine, it was a void that spoke. This was my true crime: not theft, but a communion they could not see.

The Magister’s authority, built on the visible, the categorical, the nameable, faltered before this. He could punish me for losing a page. He could not punish me for seeing a ghost. To do so would be to admit the ghost was real.

“A blank page,” he announced to the chapter, his voice regaining its steel, but it was a different alloy now, brittle and cold. “Brother Sebastian, in the zeal of his work, has mistaken a scrap of unused vellum for a unique text. His judgment is clouded. His mind is fatigued from the close, foul work of culling.” He folded the oilcloth over the page, hiding it. “He has allowed substance to be dictated by… imagination. This is a grave failure of a scribe’s primary faculty: discernment.”

He walked back to me, the bundled page in his hand. He placed it against my chest, where it had lain. The cold returned, a homecoming, a seeping relief that was more terrible than any flame.

“You are relieved of your duties in the scriptorium,” he said, his eyes locked on mine. They were no longer the eyes of a judge sentencing a thief. They were the eyes of a physician isolating a plague carrier. “You will go to the infirmary. You will rest. You will be bled to balance your humors. You will pray for clarity. And this…” he tapped the bundle, “…this *nothing* will remain with you. A reminder of the cost of seeing what is not there. When you can look upon it and see only a blank page, you may return to your ink and your pens. Not before.”

It was not the punishment I had feared. It was worse. It was erasure. My vocation, my identity as a listener to voices, was suspended on the condition that I become blind. I was to be cured of my sight. The ghost text was to be my prison and my physician.

The hands released me. The community’s gaze turned away, the scandal dissolving into the quieter, more comfortable tragedy of a brother’s breaking mind. I was no longer a thief. I was a melancholic. A man who heard whispers in blank parchment.

I was alone in a crowded room, holding the seed of frost against my heart. I had faced my fear—the structure of my world, the Magister’s law—and I had not been shattered by it. I had been hollowed out by it. My great failure was not the theft, but the failure to make them see. My devastating loss was my own voice, my authority, my place. I had undergone a symbolic death. Brother Sebastian, the scribe, the gardener of words, was gone. In his place was a vacant page, waiting for a text no one else could see.

***

The infirmary was not a place of healing. It was a place of quietude. A room where the noisy, broken things of the monastery were set aside to muffle their own clatter. It smelled of stale rushes, of a faint, sour medicinal tang, and beneath it, the sweet-sick odor of slow decay.

Brother Infirmarian was a man of few words and many leeches. He listened to the Magister’s instructions with a slow nod, his eyes on my face as if reading a mild but interesting symptom. “Fatigue of the mind,” he murmured. “The black bile ascends. We will lower it.”

My cell was a niche off the main room, containing a cot, a stool, and a small, high window that showed a sliver of sky the color of a bruise. My world had shrunk to this stone throat. They left me my habit. They left me the page.

When the door closed, and the lock slid home—a soft, final sound—I sat on the cot and unfolded the oilcloth. The white vellum lay in my lap. The light from the high window was weak. I did not need to hold it up. I could feel the ghost writing now, a vibration in my fingertips, a subtle pull behind my eyes. It was not reading. It was recognition.

I traced my finger over the surface. Smooth. Perfect. To anyone else, it was empty. To me, it was dense with un-writing. The angular, spidery script was a negative pressure. It did not say. It *unsaid*.

I lay back, the page on my chest, and let the cold root spread. It was in my mind now, a tendril of silence threading through the memory of my own life. I saw the scriptorium not as a place of light and order, but as a catacomb of dead voices, their arguments and passions just chatter over a deeper stillness. I saw the Magister’s face, a text of rigid doctrine, and I saw the fear in it when the doctrine met something it could not name. My own memories began to feel thin, scraped clean, the important parts faded to ghostliness, the trivial details overwritten and bold.

This was the text’s lesson. Its heresy was not against God, but against memory itself. Against identity. It proposed a world where the under-text was never truly gone, and the overwriting was a temporary, frantic illusion. We were all palimpsests. Our faith, our sins, our selves—just a recent, fragile layer over something older and hungrier. A void that remembered being full.

The bloodletting came at dusk. Brother Infirmarian’s hands were deft. The lancet pinched, then a hot trickle down my arm into a bronze bowl. “To relieve the pressure of the thoughts,” he said. I watched my blood, a dark, living ink, pool in the vessel. It was a unique voice, leaving me. I thought of the ghost text drinking it, the white vellum soaking up red. The idea was not horrifying. It was fitting.

Days blurred. They fed me thin gruel and bitter teas. They bled me again. I slept and woke to the same slice of sky, each day a little paler, a little colder. The ghost text was my only companion. I did not try to read it. I let it read me. It unsaid my vows. It unscratched my certainties. It made a void of Sebastian, and in that void, something else began to stir.

The Magister visited once. He stood in the doorway, not entering the cell. He looked at the page in my hands, then at my face.
“Do you see it yet?” he asked. “The blank page?”

“I see the page,” I answered, my voice rusted from disuse.

“And?”

“It is not blank. It is full of the labor of its making. The lime that cleaned it. The pumice that smoothed it. The knife that scraped it. The hand that held the knife. The sheep that gave its skin. The grass the sheep ate. The field that grew the grass. The rain that fed the field.” I was speaking the ghost text’s logic, the heresy of infinite, meaningless regress. “It is only blank if you stop looking.”

He stared at me, and I saw the physician’s fear solidify into a diagnosis. I was not getting better. The cure was revealing the disease to be terminal. He left without another word. The lock slid home again. I was a failed experiment. They would leave me here until the silence I carried either killed me or became my only language.

***

The change came not with a sound, but with a subtraction.

It was deep night. The infirmary was a pit of shadows. The slice of sky in my window was black, starless. The cold from the page had settled into my bones, a permanent winter. I was floating in the void of myself, the ghost text’s silent sermon the only thing that felt real.

Then, the silence changed.

It was not broken. It deepened. It became *active*. The cold against my chest intensified, not a root, but a mouth. A suction.

And I heard it. Not with my ears. In the hollows the text had carved in my mind.

It was not a word. It was the memory of a word. The echo of a sound that had been scraped away. It was the *un-saying* of a name.

A name I knew.

*Bernart.*

A crack in the void. A single, angular letter of sound, negative and sharp. It was the name from the first line I had murdered on my own tired parchment. *De moi doleros vos chant.* Bernart de Ventadorn. The troubadour. The first voice I had tried and failed to copy.

The ghost text was not speaking to me. It was speaking *through* me. Using the scraped-clean vellum of my memory. It was unsaying the names that anchored my world.

Another. *Magister.* The title dissolved into the silence, leaving only the shape of the fear it once held.

*Sebastian.*

My own name. Unsaid. It was not an erasure. It was a revelation. Without the name, what was left? A collection of sensations. The smell of ink. The feel of vellum. The cold hunger of the page. The memory of a theft. A will that had chosen to preserve a void. The name had been the overwriting. The *I* that remained beneath was the ghost. The under-text.

I sat up. The room was pitch, but I saw perfectly. I saw the texture of the darkness. I saw the memory of light in the stones. I was not Sebastian, the failed scribe. I was the one who had stolen the silence. That was my first, my only true act. The core text.

The lock on the door was iron, but it was also a word. A concept. *Confinement*. The ghost text unsaid it. The mechanism remained, but its meaning dissolved. I stood. I walked to the door. I placed my hand on the cold iron. It was just a shape. A arrangement of metal. I pulled.

The lock, its purpose unmade, yielded with a soft, tired click.

The door opened onto the sleeping infirmary. Brother Infirmarian snored by the banked fire. I walked past him, a shadow in a world of shadows. I was not sneaking. I was passing through. I was the void moving through the solid, temporary things.

The cloisters were empty, washed in moonlight so bright it looked like frost. I carried the page in my hand, no longer hiding it. It was no longer a secret. It was a standard. A flag of nothing.

I went not to the scriptorium, nor to the gate. I went to the church.

The great door groaned, a profound, wooden sigh. The interior was vast, a cavern of swallowed night, pierced by shafts of moonlight through the high clerestory. They fell on the stone floor like pillars of bone. The altar was a shadow at the far end. The air smelled of cold stone and old incense, the memory of prayer.

I walked down the nave, my footsteps the only sound in God’s own silent throat. I stopped before the altar. I laid the ghost page upon it, the white vellum a shocking absence on the dark wood.

I knelt. Not to pray. To witness.

The moon shifted. A beam, pure and cold, fell directly upon the page.

And there, in that silver, unforgiving light, the ghost text finally showed itself to my eyes. Not as faint writing. As a *removal*. The vellum was not blank. Where the angular script lay, the very surface of the skin was gone. Not scraped. *Unmade*. It was a labyrinth of infinitesimal, perfect voids, letters carved out of existence itself. They did not reflect light. They drank it. They were the memory of text made physical as absence.

I could read it now. Not with language, but with understanding. It was a single, endless sentence. A chant of uncreation. It did not argue against God. It simply unwrote the words *God*, *world*, *soul*, *sin*, *faith*. It was the slow, patient echo of the knife that scrapes, the pumice that smooths. It was the heresy of the final, empty page at the end of all books.

This was the reward for my ordeal. Not an object. A knowledge. The knowledge that I had been right. This was the most unique voice. It was the voice of the vellum itself, screaming in the silence of its own making. It was the truth beneath the Magister’s tidy metaphors. The fire did not just burn chaff. It also burned, and would eventually burn, the wheat. The scythe cut everything. This text was the scythe.

I had earned the right to see it. By stealing it. By being broken by it. By having my own name unsaid.

I took the page from the altar. The moonbeam held it for a moment longer, caressing its nothingness. Then the light moved on.

I had overcome not by defeating my enemy, but by understanding that he and I were both texts, awaiting the same scrape of the knife. I had survived the symbolic death of Sebastian to become something else: the keeper of the silence. The gardener of the void.

I left the church. The monastery slept on, a dream of stone and rule. I walked through the cloister, past the scriptorium door. I did not enter. That life was the overwriting. It was already fading.

I went to the gatehouse. The porter, a young brother named Luc, dozed on his stool. He started awake as I approached.

“Brother Sebastian? The Infirmarian said you were—”

“I am released,” I said, and my voice carried the cold of the page, a quiet authority that was not my own. It was the authority of the inevitable. He heard it. He blinked, confused, but the will to challenge it had been unsaid before it could form.

He opened the small postern door within the great gate.

The world outside was a tapestry of black and silver. A road, a strip of pale dirt, wound away into a landscape of sleeping fields and distant, dark hills. The air was vast, sharp with the smell of turned earth and freedom. It was the smell of the meadow in the vellum, remembered.

I stood on the threshold. I was a thief, a melancholic, a broken scribe. I was also the only one who could hear the whisper of the un-writing at the heart of all things. I had my page. I had my truth. It was a frost that would kill any living word it touched.

I stepped across.

The door shut behind me with a solid, final thud. I was outside the text of the monastery. The only text left was the one in my hand, and the one it was writing on me.

I looked back once. The walls were high and black against the sky. A prison. A scriptorium. A memory already growing faint.

I turned and walked down the road, the ghost text cold and alive in my hand. I had no destination. But I had a purpose. I was the vessel for the unique voice. I would carry its silent, hungry sermon into the world of noise. I would find the places where the overwriting was thin, and I would let the void beneath be heard.

And I would learn, in time, what happened when a blank page decided to write back.



---

# Chapter 6

## **Chapter 6**

The reward was a dry place, and we were parched.

It was a barn on the edge of a sleeping village, a cathedral of dust and stacked hay. The air was thick with the sweet, decaying smell of last summer’s harvest and the warm, living scent of the few cattle lowing softly in their stalls. To us, it was a palace. We had paid for it in blood and silence, and now we claimed its emptiness as our spoils. The farmer, a man with eyes like chipped flint, had been persuaded not by our words—we had few, and those were blunt—but by the weight of the silver coin I placed in his palm. It was cold, a little piece of forged order. He took it, his fingers closing over the lie of its value, and vanished into the night, leaving us to his kingdom of shadows and straw.

Elara sat with her back against a great, rough-hewn post, her bow unstrung across her knees like a sleeping companion. She was cleaning the blood from her nails with the point of her knife, a slow, meticulous ritual. Tomas, the broad-shouldered carter who had become our unlikely shield, was already asleep, his breath a steady, sawing rhythm from a mound of hay. The boy, Piers, was feeding apple cores to one of the cows, whispering to it as if sharing a secret. We were four. We had been seven when we left the city gates.

The victory was a shallow cup, and I had already tasted the dregs. It was not in the retrieved chest—a plain, iron-banded thing that sat now in the center of the threshing floor, a mute altar. The victory was in the stillness. The absence of pursuit. The granting of this pause. It was a blank space in the narrative of flight, and like all blank spaces, I distrusted it. I could feel the vellum of this moment, stretched thin and waiting for the next wound of ink.

I leaned against the ladder to the loft, the ghost page a cool, flat presence against my chest, beneath my tunic. It was quieter here, sated. It had fed on the violence in the forest pass, on the unsaid things that hung in the air between our ragged band. It had fed on the man whose throat Elara had opened with an arrow’s fletching—a close, wet, wordless end. The page drank the silence that followed such acts, the great, gasping void where a life’s story had been. It was digesting.

“We should open it,” Tomas said, his voice a gravelly rumble from the hay. He hadn’t been asleep, only resting his eyes. They were open now, reflecting the guttering light of our single lantern.

“We know what’s in it,” Elara said, not looking up from her work. “Silver. The Baron’s payment. Our price.”

“*Our* price,” Tomas echoed, the word tasting unfamiliar. “Aye. But I’d see it. I’d feel the weight of it in my hand. To know the shape of the thing we bought.”

*Bought.* A transaction. His soul, and the souls of two others now cooling in the forest loam, exchanged for a chest of stamped metal. It was a clean arithmetic. I envied him the simplicity of it. My own economy was more obscure. I had traded a monastery’s silence for a different, more hungry one. I had traded the overwriting of God for the unwriting of this… entity, this absence I carried. I was not certain I had received the better bargain.

“Open it, then,” I said.

My voice was not my own. It was the voice from the gatehouse, carrying the cold, flat authority of the inevitable. It was the voice of the page. They heard it. Elara’s knife stilled. Tomas sat up, hay clinging to his hair like a crown of dry chaff.

He fetched a pry-bar from the farmer’s tools and set to the hasp. The sound was a screech of protest that tore the fabric of our fragile peace. The cattle shifted in their stalls. Piers flinched, pressing his cheek against the cow’s warm flank. The iron bands groaned, then yielded with a final, metallic sigh.

Tomas lifted the lid.

The lantern light fell in, liquid and gold, and spilled over the contents. It was not silver.

It was books.

Five of them, bound in dark, bruised leather, laid neatly in the straw-padded interior. No jewels, no coin, no mercantile promise. Just the quiet, dense weight of word and thought.

Tomas stared, his big hands hanging useless at his sides. “What trick is this?”

Elara was on her feet now, her knife forgotten. She came forward, her movements those of a hunter approaching a strange, trapped animal. She reached in and lifted one volume. It was small, thick. She opened it. The pages whispered a secret to the air. She could not read the words, but she could feel their intent. Her face, usually a mask of pragmatic ferocity, softened into confusion, then something like fear.

“It’s prayers,” she said, her voice hushed. “Latin, I think. Liturgies.”

“A chest of prayers?” Tomas’s laugh was short, brutal. “We died for a chest of prayers? The Baron plays a cruel game.”

But I understood. A cold clarity washed through me, the frost of the page spreading from my chest to my fingertips. I stepped forward. “Not a game. A message.”

I took the book from Elara. The leather was supple, expensive. The pages were fine vellum, not paper. The script was a beautiful, disciplined Carolingian minuscule, the work of a master scribe. I knew this hand. I had aspired to it once, in another life. It was from the scriptorium of the very monastery I had fled.

*De profundis clamavi ad te, Domine…*

Out of the depths, I have cried to you, O Lord.

This was no ordinary devotional. This was a Psalter, a knight’s Psalter, meant for the saddlebag of a pious nobleman. Worth more than its weight in silver to the right buyer. Worth infinitely more as a sign.

“He knew,” I said, the words leaving my lips like breath on a winter morning. “The Baron. He didn’t hire us to retrieve stolen silver. He hired us to retrieve stolen *property*. Property stolen from *him*. This is his library. His piety. His legitimacy. Someone didn’t just rob him. They mocked him. They took the symbols of his grace.”

“So we fetched his books,” Tomas spat. “The price remains the same. He’ll pay in proper coin.”

“Will he?” I asked, looking from Tomas’s fury to Elara’s dawning comprehension. “We have seen his treasure. We know its nature. We are no longer mere mercenaries. We are confidants. Witnesses to a humiliation. Men pay to erase such witnesses.”

The silence that followed was different from before. It was not the silence of respite. It was the silence of a trap realizing it has been sprung. The celebratory air curdled, becoming the thin, sharp atmosphere of consequence. Our reward had transformed. It was no longer a destination, but a provocation. The chest sat there, its open mouth full of silent, shouting words.

Piers began to cry, softly, hopelessly. He was mourning the two men—his uncle, and a friend—who had died for a handful of parchment. He was mourning the silver that would never come. Elara walked back to her post and did not sit, but stood staring into the dark recesses of the barn, as if seeing the future there, and finding it armed.

Tomas kicked the chest, a hollow, wooden thud. “Then we take them. We find a seller. A rich abbot. We get our price.”

“And bring the Baron’s wrath down upon us instead of the original thief’s?” Elara shook her head. “We are caught, Tomas. The parchment has caught us.”

I held the Psalter. The words were a familiar topography, a landscape I had copied a hundred times. But now, they felt alien. They were overwritten, every sacred syllable a layer of paint on a rotting wall. Beneath them, I could feel the ghost page stir, a sympathetic vibration. It recognized this. It recognized the heavy, solemn weight of authorized text. It wanted to scrape it clean.

I closed the book. The sound was a verdict.

“We mourn our dead now,” I said. “That, at least, is a true transaction.”

***
