We buried nothing, for we had no bodies to inter. We had only their absence, which we laid in the soil of our memory.

It was Tomas who began it. He found a skin of poor wine the farmer had left, took a long pull, and then poured a libation onto the packed earth of the barn floor. “For Marten,” he grunted. “Who loved a good joke. Sorry this one was on you, brother.”

Elara took the skin. She did not drink. She simply held it for a long moment, her eyes closed. “For Cade,” she said. Her voice was tight, a wire under tension. “Who watched my back. I have failed to watch his.” She tipped the skin, the wine a dark, sacrificial stain.

They looked at me. I was the stranger, the scribe who had stumbled into their company with his quiet and his odd certainty. I had not known the men. I had only seen them die—one with a crossbow bolt in his eye, the other with the gurgling, red surprise of Elara’s close work. Their stories had ended mid-sentence.

I took the skin. The ghost page was cold against my skin. I thought of the vellum I had ruined in the scriptorium, scraped too thin, fit only for kindling or for wrapping fish. These men were now stories for the kindling. Their narratives had been burned for the brief, warm lie of our purpose.

“For the words they will not speak,” I said, and my voice was the dry rustle of that final vellum. “For the stories overwritten by the dirt and the dark. May the silence that takes them be a kinder scribe than the world was.”

I poured the last of the wine. It was not enough. It was never enough. The ritual was a blank space, an empty line on the parchment of the night. We tried to fill it with meaning, but the meaning leaked out, leaving only the stain and the hollow feeling.

Piers, curled in the hay, cried himself into a fitful sleep. His dreams would be the shroud we could not provide.

Elara finally sat, her energy spent. She looked at the open chest, then at me. “You knew,” she said, not as an accusation, but as a weary observation. “Not about the books. But about this. The cost. The after.”

“I knew that every text has a shadow,” I said. “Every ‘yes’ contains the ghost of its ‘no’. We achieved the yes. Now we meet the no.”

“And the thing you carry?” she asked. Her eyes were sharp, missing nothing. “The page you guard like a second heart. Is it a ‘yes’ or a ‘no’?”

“It is the margin,” I said. “The space around the words where the truth bleeds through.”

She did not understand. But she accepted the answer as the only one I had to give. We sat in the aftermath, the barn holding our grief and our disillusionment like a patient, indifferent vessel. The reward’s true nature was not the books, but this: the revelation of our own price, paid in a currency of doubt. We were no longer a company bound by a goal. We were a collection of individuals, stranded together on an island of bad choices, watching the tide of consequence come in.

***

The tide arrived before dawn.

It came not with a roar, but with the careful, professional silence of men who have done this before. There was no shouted challenge, no thunder of hoofbeats. Just the subtle, wrong sounds from outside: the too-quiet cessation of the crickets, the soft, metallic *shink* of a blade clearing its scabbard, the crunch of a careful foot on gravel.

Elara was on her feet in an instant, bow strung, an arrow nocked from pure instinct. Tomas was up, hefting his cudgel, his face a mask of sleepy rage. Piers whimpered, and I clamped a hand over his mouth, pulling him into the deeper shadow behind a grain barrel.

They were at the doors. Not attempting stealth now. A heavy, mailed fist hammered against the wood. “Open! In the name of Baron Gui!”

The voice was bored, officious. It was the voice of the inevitable, arriving on schedule.

Tomas looked at Elara, then at me, his eyes asking the only question left: *Fight or flight?*

Flight was a memory. The barn had one large door and a small side door near the stalls. They would be covered. We were parchment in a frame, awaiting the knife.

“Open it,” I said to Tomas.

He hesitated, then threw the bar and hauled one of the great doors open a few feet.

The man who stood there was not the Baron. He was a captain, his surcoat bearing the Baron’s boar emblem. He was flanked by four men-at-arms, their weapons ready but not yet raised. They were not here for a slaughter. They were here for a retrieval.

“The chest,” the captain said, his eyes scanning the dim interior, counting us, assessing the threat. He saw Elara’s drawn bow and his expression didn’t change. He’d seen worse. “You have it. Hand it over, and you may go.”

“Our payment,” Tomas growled. “The Baron promised silver.”

“The Baron,” the captain said, stepping inside, his men flowing in behind him to flank the doorway, “is aware of his promise. He is also aware that the contents of the chest are… sensitive. He prefers to settle accounts personally. You will come with us. The chest will come with us. All will be settled at the castle.”

It was a lie so transparent it was almost an insult. To go with them was to enter a different text, one written by the Baron in a dungeon cell. Our stories would end with a question, a confession, a final, short sentence of steel.

Elara’s bowstring creaked. “We settle now. Our dead are paid for. The chest is yours. We walk away.”

The captain’s smile was thin. “The woman has spirit. But no. You see the books. You know what was taken. This is not a simple robbery. It is an affair of state. Your presence is required.”

*Affair of state.* The words hung in the dusty air. The stolen Psalters were not just piety; they were politics. The Baron’s claim to his lands, his alliance with the Church, his very identity as a righteous lord—all were bound in those pages. Their theft was a challenge. Our recovery of them made us part of the story. And the Baron, like any author, would want to control the narrative. He would edit us out.

The ghost page trembled against my chest. It felt the tension, the web of spoken and unspoken threats. It fed on the silent calculation in Elara’s eyes, the suppressed fury in Tomas’s stance, the cold professionalism of the captain. This was a moment of overwriting. The Baron’s men were here to impose their text, their ending, upon us.

I stepped out from the shadows, my hand on Piers’s shoulder, pushing him gently behind the barrel.

“Captain,” I said, and again, that cold, flat tone used me for its instrument. “You have your mandate. We have ours. The chest is there.” I gestured to the open box. “Take it. Report that we fled in the night, that you found only the spoils. Your lord has his property. His humiliation is reversed. The story ends cleanly.”

The captain looked at me properly for the first time. He saw a man in a stained scribe’s robe, thin, no obvious weapon. He saw the calm, which he mistook for weakness. “And why would I do that? My orders are to bring you in.”

“Because,” I said, taking another step forward, placing myself between him and my companions, “the story you propose has more characters than necessary. It has complications. Witnesses. Loose threads. The story I propose is simpler. More elegant. It has a satisfied lord and a missing, unimportant band of vagabonds. It is the story that requires the least ink.”

I was speaking his language now. The language of administration. Of clean reports and manageable truths.

He considered. His eyes flicked to the chest, to his well-armed men, to our ragged four. The balance of power was absurdly in his favor. Yet, a fight, even a short one, could see the chest damaged, or a lucky arrow find his throat. Taking us by force was messy. Letting us vanish was… efficient.

“The boy stays,” the captain said abruptly, pointing at Piers. “A guarantee of your silence. You three disappear. If word of this reaches any ear, the boy dies in ways that will become a song of horror.”

Piers let out a choked sob. Elara’s aim shifted infinitesimally, the arrowhead now pointing at the captain’s face. “No.”

“Then we do it my way,” the captain said, his hand rising to signal his men.

The overwriting was about to begin. The Baron’s text—of capture, interrogation, disposal—was about to be inscribed upon us, upon this night. I could feel its weight, its leaden certainty.

And the ghost page within my tunic… pushed back.

It was not a physical force. It was a negation. A sudden, localized vacuum of intention. The lantern light seemed to dim, not in brightness, but in significance. The captain’s commanding posture wavered, just for a heartbeat. His order died on his lips. He blinked, as if trying to remember the thread of his own thought. His men shuffled, their certainty diluted.

The page was hungry. It was feeding on the *authority* in the room, on the imposed narrative. It was scraping it thin.

In that hiccup of the inevitable, Elara moved.

She did not shoot the captain. She shot the lantern.

The arrow shattered the clay reservoir. Burning oil splashed down over the dry straw piled by the door, over the captain’s boots, over the chest itself. Fire, the great unwriter, erupted with a soft, hungry gasp.

Chaosis a blank page. It accepts any story.

The captain shouted, stumbling back from the sudden flame. His men broke formation, batting at sparks. The barn, a cathedral of tinder, began to whisper its own crackling sermon.

“Now!” Tomas roared, and he was a bull, charging not at the men, but at the side wall of the barn where the old wood was rotten. He hit it with his shoulder, a sound of splintering protest.

“The chest!” the captain yelled, but his command was lost in the smoke and panic.

Elara had Piers by the arm, dragging him toward the hole Tomas was making. She looked back at me. “Sebastian!”

I stood amidst the blooming fire, watching the flames lick at the chest. The books within would blacken, curl, become ash. The Baron’s legitimacy, his political text, was being consumed. It was a fitting end. A cleaner erasure than any dungeon.

But the page against my chest was ice-cold, screaming without sound. It did not want the books burned. It wanted them *empty*. It wanted their words gone but their vessels intact. The fire was too final, too total. It was not unwriting; it was annihilation.

I acted without thought. I lunged through the heat, grabbed the iron handle of the chest, and hauled it back from the heart of the fire. The metal was hot. The smell of scorched leather filled my nostrils. One volume had caught, its pages glowing like angry jewels before crumbling.

Tomas had broken through. Cold, clean night air poured into the burning barn. Elara shoved Piers through the gap and turned, firing two more arrows into the smoky confusion to cover me.

I dragged the chest, a dead weight of contested meaning, across the floor. Tomas reached in, his big hand closing around my forearm, and hauled me and my burden through the shattered wall into the shocking cold of the outside.

We ran. Not toward the road, but into the field behind the barn, toward a copse of trees that hunched like conspirators against the sky. The barn was a roaring pyre behind us, painting the world in pulses of orange and black. There were shouts, but no pursuit. The captain’s priority was the fire, his men, survival.

We reached the trees and collapsed, gasping, into the damp bracken. The chest lay beside me, smoldering gently, its iron bands hot to the touch. Piers was weeping openly. Tomas leaned against a tree, sucking in great lungfuls of air. Elara watched the burning barn, her face a stark study in flame-light and shadow.

I looked down at the chest. I had saved it. I had chosen, in the crucial moment, to preserve the vessel of the overwritten text. I had chosen the potential of the blank page over the finality of the fire.

The ghost page was calm now, nestled against me. It had gotten what it wanted. A reprieve. More words to someday scrape away.

I had made my choice. We had not retreated with our reward. We had pressed on, seizing it fully, and in doing so, had bound ourselves to it more completely than ever. We were no longer its retrievers. We were its accomplices. We had stolen the Baron’s reclaimed property from the Baron’s own men. We had burned his barn. We were not vagabonds to be forgotten. We were enemies to be hunted.

The celebration was ashes. The consequence was upon us. The new threat was not emerging; it had been born from the cinders of our own actions.

And as the first grey light of dawn began to unwrite the stars, I opened the scorched chest. I lifted out the topmost book, its cover blistered. I opened it to a random page. The beautiful script was marred by a water stain, the ink blurred into a Rorschach blot of meaningless beauty.

Beneath the stain, beneath the faded Latin, I could almost see it. The true vellum. The blankness. Waiting.

We had our reward. It was a chest of questions, and a world of men who would now demand answers with sharpened steel. I had my purpose. It was colder, and hungrier, than ever.

And on the wind, carried from the direction of the Baron’s distant castle, I thought I heard the first, faint sound of the hounds being released.



---

# Chapter 7

## **Chapter 7**

The forest was a cathedral of accusation, and we were its only congregation.

We moved through the grey dawn not as fugitives, but as a procession of guilt. The bracken beneath our feet was a chorus of whispers with every step. The chest, slung between Tomas and Piers on a cloak-pole, was our reliquary, its scorched wood a blasphemous contrast to the clean, damp smell of pine and leaf-rot. My own skin felt like vellum stretched too tight over a faulty frame, every sense scraped raw. I could still taste the smoke. It had woven itself into the wool of my tunic, into the ghost page’s faint, papery scent, into the back of my throat—a permanent, acrid hymn to what we had done.

Elara walked point, a silent, sharp-edged shadow. She did not look back at the chest. She looked ahead, into the lattice of trees, listening for the hymn we all feared: the baying of hounds. Piers’s weeping had subsided into a hitching breath, a rhythm of pure fear. Tomas’s face was set in a grimace that had long since ceased to be an expression and had become a fact of his bones.

“We cannot go back to the road,” Tomas said, his voice a low rasp that seemed to startle the quiet. “They’ll have riders on every track before full light.”

“We don’t go to the road,” I said. The decision had formed in me as the barn collapsed into a skeleton of flame—cold, clear, and inevitable. “We go to the river. The Eirian.”

Elara glanced back, her eyes catching a shard of light. “The Baron’s mills are on the Eirian. His watchtowers.”

“His jurisdiction ends where the river deepens, a day’s walk north. The water is our path out of his ordinary world.” The words tasted of irony. There was no ‘ordinary world’ to return to. We had burned it down. We were carrying its ashes in a box.

“A day’s walk with this,” Tomas grunted, adjusting his grip on the pole. The chest swayed, a pendulum measuring our doom.

“Then we walk,” I said.

***

The reward was a toothache in my mind.

It throbbed with every jolt of the chest, a dull, insistent pressure behind my eyes. I had chosen the blank page. The potential. But potential is a form of hunger, and this hunger was a living thing in the chest, gnawing at its constraints. The ghost page was a placid leech against my chest, sated for now, dreaming its dreams of erasure. But the books… the books were different. They were not blank. They were palimpsests, their truths buried under layers of sanctioned lies. They were not potential. They were a specific, dangerous knowledge. And we were their carriers.

By mid-morning, the celebratory shock had worn off entirely, leaving only the metallic aftertaste of consequence. We were a wound moving through the forest, and the world was beginning to sense it.

The first sign was the silence.

The birdsong, which had been a scattered, drowsy melody at dawn, ceased. It did not taper. It was cut. One moment, the trees were alive with the gossip of finches; the next, there was only the wind in the high pines and the crush of our own passage. We stopped as one, frozen in the act of motion. Elara held up a clenched fist. We listened to the nothing.

It was Piers who heard it first. A soft, wet snuffling, thirty paces to our left. The sound of something large, methodically tasting the air.

We did not speak. We did not breathe. We became still as the trees, hoping our scent would be lost in the damp earth and pine resin. The snuffling came closer, a slow, deliberate exploration. Through a screen of hawthorn, I saw a flash of dun-brown flank, the heavy, muscular swing of a shoulder. A boar. Not a forest sow, but a barrel-bodied, tusked patriarch, its hide scarred from fights and thick with wiry bristles. It paused, its small, dark eye seeming to look directly at our hiding place. It was not hunting us. It was assessing a disturbance. A wrongness. The wrongness of the chest, of the fire on our clothes, of the fear-sweat that poured from us.

It lowered its head, gave a low, guttural chuff of displeasure, and turned away, melting back into the undergrowth. The forest had judged us. Found us alien. The ordinary creatures were withdrawing, leaving us to the hunt that was surely coming.

“It knows,” Piers whispered, his voice trembling. “The very woods know what we’ve done.”

“Quiet,” Elara hissed, but the truth of it was in her eyes. We were no longer part of this landscape. We were a stain upon it.

We pushed on, the silence now a heavier burden than the chest. The river was our only liturgy. We followed the gradual slope of the land downward, our ears straining for the murmur of water over stone. It was a sound that promised motion, escape, a cleansing. A false promise, perhaps, but the only one we had.

The sound, when it came, was not water.

It was a horn. A single, blunt note, punched through the forest canopy from the south-west. It was not a hunting call. It was a declaration. A statement of territory reclaimed. The Baron’s men were not searching. They were deploying.

The chase was not an event. It was a condition. It had begun the moment the first spark took hold in the barn’s thatch, and it would only end when we were caught or when we crossed into some other lord’s misery.

“Run,” Tomas said. It was not a suggestion. It was the last, logical verb.

We ran. The chest became a malevolent spirit, catching on roots, banging against trees, threatening to spill its precious, damning contents. Piers sobbed with each step, his breath a ragged prayer. Elara flitted ahead, a dagger now in her hand, choosing the path of least resistance through the thickening brush. The land fell away more steeply, and the sound of the river grew—a real, rushing counterpoint to the silent pursuit behind us.

We burst from the tree line onto a narrow, rocky shelf overlooking the Eirian. The river was wider than I’d hoped, a swift, grey-green serpent churning over a bed of moss-slick stone. On the far bank, the forest climbed again, dark and deep. Freedom was a hundred feet of cold, furious water away.

“There!” Piers cried, pointing upstream.

A rider. Then two. Emerging from the trees a bowshot distant, picking their way along the bank. They wore the Baron’s livery—a red chevron on a field of dun. They had not seen us yet. The river’s roar and the high bank shielded us.

“The chest,” I said. “We float it. Use it as a raft.”

Tomas stared at me as if I’d gone mad. “It’s iron-bound oak! It’ll sink like an anvil!”

“The books will float. They’ll be ruined, but they’ll float. We must empty it.”
It was a desecration. To treat these careful, overwritten pages as mere buoyant material. But the horn sounded again, closer. The riders were turning, their gaze sweeping the riverbank.

We fell upon the chest. The hasp was hot, warped from the fire, but Tomas wedged his belt-knife beneath it and heaved. With a shriek of tortured iron, it sprang open. The smell that billowed out was complex: charred leather, wet vellum, the sweet-sour tang of ancient ink, and beneath it all, that faint, clean scent of the true substrate. The blankness.

We did not look at the pages. We could not afford to see the beautiful, blurred scripts, the water-stained illuminations. We grabbed armfuls. The books were surprisingly heavy, their vellum pages thick and damp. We piled them on the ground, a pathetic, sodden treasure. The ghost page on my chest gave a faint, approving tremor, as if delighted by this further step towards dissolution.

“Now the chest,” I said. “Shove it in. It will draw their eye.”

Together, we manhandled the empty, smoldering crate to the edge of the bank and pushed it over. It tumbled, struck the water with a loud crash, and was immediately seized by the current. It spun, a black, obvious shape, and began its swift journey downstream.

“The riders will follow it,” Elara said, her eyes hard. “It buys us minutes.”

“Now us,” I said. “Each of you, take a book. Hold it to your chest. It will keep you from sinking. Swim for the far bank, downstream of where we enter.”

Piers looked at the turbulent water, then at the armful of soaked, priceless text in his hands. His fear of drowning warred with his awe of the object. Awe lost. He clutched the book like a child.

We went into the water. The cold was a physical blow, driving the air from my lungs. The current was a hand around my ankles, pulling me under. The book I held, a massive psalter, was both anchor and lifeline. It wanted to sink, but the air trapped in its soaked pages gave it a stubborn buoyancy. I kicked, fought the drag, and aimed for the dark line of the far bank.

The world narrowed to the roar of water, the burning in my muscles, the solid, sodden weight in my arms. I did not look back. I heard a shout, muffled by the river’s voice. The riders had seen the chest. Or perhaps they had seen us. It didn’t matter. The water was our chaos, our temporary salvation.

My feet found purchase on slick, rounded stone. I stumbled, fell, and crawled the last few feet onto the opposite bank, vomiting river water. Tomas was already there, hauling Piers up by his collar. Elara emerged like an otter, silent and efficient, her dagger still gripped in her teeth, her book held high.

We had crossed. We had our reward, now a collection of waterlogged, bloated codices. We were on the wrong side of the Baron’s authority. But as I lay on the stones, gasping, I looked back across the churning water.

Two riders had dismounted where we had entered. One was pointing at our discarded pile of books, the few we’d left behind in our panic. The other was looking directly at me. He raised his horn to his lips. This time, the note was different. Shorter, sharper, repeated in a triplet. A call to others. A signal that the prey had been sighted, and the direction was known.

The chase was not over. It had simply found its rhythm.

***

We did not stop to rest. We were creatures of momentum now. The river had stolen our body’s warmth and given us a desperate, shivering energy in return. We fled north, keeping the Eirian within earshot to our right, a guide and a threat. The books were a catastrophe. Their bindings were splitting, pages peeling away like bloated skin. We carried them as gently as we could, cradling them against our bodies, but with every step, we were losing words. Ink was bleeding, pigments washing into meaningless rainbows. We were not saving knowledge. We were presiding over its final, watery un-writing.

By late afternoon, the landscape began to change. The dense, managed forest of the Baron’s domain gave way to older, wilder wood. The trees were larger, their trunks thick with moss, the canopy so high and thick it created a perpetual twilight. The undergrowth was thinner. The path, what little there was, was made by deer and boar, not men. We had crossed into a borderland, a place between authorities.

It was here, in the cathedral quiet of the primeval wood, that the central conflict ceased to be an external force and rooted itself inside me.

We found a shallow cave, little more than a scoop in a sandstone cliff, curtained by the roots of a fallen beech. It was dry. It was hidden. It was a place to stop, to finally look at what we had nearly died for.

We built a tiny, smokeless fire of dry heartwood, just enough to cast a faint glow and push back the deep chill. Our clothes steamed. In the flickering light, we laid out the rescued books. They were ruins. The beautiful Caroline minuscule of a chronicle had run into a grey fog. A Book of Hours had its vibrant miniatures of saints blurred into ghostly, weeping faces. The vellum pages were cockled and stiff, their surfaces a topography of disaster.

Piers began to weep again, softly this time, as he tried to gently separate pages that had fused together. “It’s all gone. It’s all for nothing.”

“No,” I said, my voice hollow. “Look closer.”

I took the chronicle from him. I held a page near the feeble heat of the fire. As the vellum warmed, the faded black ink of the Latin text seemed to recede. And beneath it, as if rising from a deep pool, other marks became visible. Fainter. Older. A different hand. A rust-brown ink, almost the color of dried blood. It was not Latin. The letters were more angular, the language utterly alien to me. The overwritten text, the official history, was washing away. The substrate was asserting itself.

The ghost page on my chest hummed, a vibration of pure, avaricious joy.

“The fire and the water,” I whispered. “They are not destroying it. They are revealing it.”

Elara leaned in, her sharp face intent. “What does it say?”

“I don’t know. It is a language before language here. A memory before history.” I looked around at their faces, lit by the dying fire. Tomas, his brute strength useless here. Piers, his reverence curdled into despair. Elara, her practicality meeting its limit. “This is the confrontation. Not with the Baron’s men. With this.” I gestured at the ruined, revealing pages. “We thought we were stealing answers. We were stealing a deeper set of questions. We cannot outrun that. The Baron hunts a chest of books. But if he knew what was truly emerging from beneath the script… his hunt would become a crusade.”

The realization was a cold stone in my gut. The final confrontation was not a battle with swords in a forest clearing. It was a choice about what to do with this awakening memory. We could try to rebury it. We could try to carry it to some imagined safe place. Or we could learn to read it.

But to learn to read it was to give it a voice. To give a buried, forgotten identity a tongue. And who knew what that voice would say? What claims it would make? What truths it would force upon a world that had carefully written over it?

“We cannot keep them all,” I said. The words cost me. “The weight will slow us to a crawl. The Baron’s net will tighten. We must choose.”

“Choose?” Tomas spat. “We almost died for this slop. You want to leave it for the crows?”

“We must choose the *key*,” I said. “One book. The one where the underlying text is clearest. The one that seems most… intentional. The rest we hide. We bury them here, in this dry cave. If we survive, we can return. If we do not, they remain lost. It is the only way.”

It was the sacrifice. Not of a life, but of certainty. Of the greedy, possessive hope of saving everything. We had to commit to a fragment, and trust that the fragment held the shape of the whole.

For hours, we worked. By the faint, dancing light, we examined every bloated page, every bleeding illumination. We were not scribes now. We were diviners, reading the scars on the vellum, seeking the pattern in the palimpsest’s decay. Piers, with his gentle hands, found it. It was not the largest book, nor the most beautifully illuminated. It was a modest volume, its original binding long since replaced with plain, worn boards. The overwritten text was a tedious treatise on land boundaries and tithes. But the water had been merciless to it. The black ink had pooled and run, and beneath it, the rust-brown script stood clear and stark. And there were not just words. There were maps. Sketches of coastlines that did not match any I knew. Stars over mountains that had no names. And in the margins, drawn with a frantic, urgent hand, were the same symbols I had seen on the ghost page: the spiral, the broken tower, the eye within the circle.

“This one,” Piers said, his voice filled with a new kind of awe. “This is the root. The rest are branches.”

We hid the other books deep in the cave’s recess, sealing them in a niche with stones and dry earth. We kept the one. It felt lighter than the entire chest, yet infinitely heavier.

As we prepared to move again in the pre-dawn gloom, the choice settled upon me, colder and hungrier than any before. I was not just a preserver anymore. I was an excavator. I was committing to unearthing a memory the world had labored to forget. I was choosing to give this buried identity a voice, knowing it would speak through me, that its truth would overwrite my own quiet life completely. The final battle would not be for my life, but for my mind. To retain my own story in the face of this older, more urgent one.

Elara touched my arm. She nodded toward the south, back across the river we could no longer see or hear. A new sound threaded through the ancient wood. Distant, but unmistakable. Not a horn.

It was the deep, bell-like voice of a hound catching a scent. Then another. A chorus.

They were across the river. They were in the wild wood. The Baron’s hunt had refined its purpose. It was no longer after thieves. It was after heresy.

I clutched the single, damp book to my chest, feeling the ghost page beneath my tunic press against it, a communion of blank and written, of question and half-revealed answer. We had made our sacrifice. We had chosen our fragment of truth.

And as we turned our faces north, into the deepest dark of the wood, I knew the confrontation had already begun. It was happening in the rust-brown script bleeding into my fingertips, in the hollow echo of the hounds’ voices, in the silent, waiting memory of the land itself. I had sought purpose. Now it sought me. It would speak, and I would have to learn, before the world’s sharpened steel arrived to silence us both, to ensure the forgotten thing stayed buried, its identity forever scraped clean from the tired, thirsty vellum of history.



---

# Chapter 8

## **Chapter 8**

The book was a battlefield, and I was losing.

It lay open on a slab of moss-cushioned granite, its damp pages a geography of ruin. The ink, once a defiant black, had bled into a spectral grey, the letters softening at their edges like shadows at noon. I could smell its surrender—a scent of wet ash and dissolved earth, mixed with the cold perfume of stone and the older, deeper smell of the wildwood itself—rot, resilience, a ghost of forgotten sunlight. We had tried to dry it by a low, smokeless fire. The heat had only set the dampness deeper, had made the vellum curl like a dying leaf. One more attempt and the knowledge would be ruined, fit only for pulp or for tinder.

The line I was trying to resurrect was simple. *Is e an t-ainm nach scriobhtar.* “It is the name that is not written.” The opening of a lament by a poet whose name this very book had erased. The rest of the page was a lagoon of sepia blooms, a continent of loss.

Elara watched the tree line, her body a taut bowstring. The hounds had fallen silent an hour ago, or we had outrun the reach of their voices. The silence was worse. It was the pause between the drawing of the bow and the flight of the arrow. The wood here was a cathedral of ancient pine, the canopy so high and thick the forest floor was in a perpetual, brown twilight. No undergrowth. Only a carpet of needles that drank sound. Our every breath was a proclamation.

“We cannot stop long,” she said, not looking at me. Her voice was the scrape of a knife on whetstone.

“I know.”

But my fingers were on the page, tracing the raised, wounded script. I was not reading. I was listening. The confrontation the cave had promised was here. Not with steel, but with silence. The memory in the book was dying under my touch, retreating from the world a second time. My purpose, so fiercely chosen, was leaking away between the fibres of the vellum. To be an excavator of a truth that crumbled at the first exposure to air was a new kind of martyrdom. It asked for my identity not in a flash of sacrifice, but in the slow, granular attrition of hope.

I closed the book. The ghost page, sewn now into the lining of my tunic, lay against my chest. It was a cool, persistent blankness. A void facing a void. A question meeting an answer that would not hold its shape.

“They are not searching randomly,” Elara murmured. She knelt, her fingers pressing into the needles. “They move in a line. A pincer. The Baron’s huntsman is a man named Caden. He thinks in patterns. He cuts a section of forest from the herd, then drives his quarry to a river or a cliff.” She lifted her fingers, showing me a crushed pine needle. “We are the quarry. The heresy is the herd.”

The word *heresy* hung in the still air. It was no longer an accusation from outside. It was the soil we stood on. It was the book in my hands. It was the blank page on my skin. I had thought the battle would be for my mind, against the memory that sought to overwrite me. I was wrong. The battle was to become a worthy vessel for it before it vanished. To let it overwrite me completely was the only victory possible. The self I had been—the careful scribe, the preserver of others’ voices—that man was already ash. The integration was not a blending, but a succession.

“Then we break the pattern,” I said. The words felt unfamiliar in my mouth, like a language long disused. “We do not run where the forest dictates. We go where it does not.”

She looked at me then, her eyes the colour of the deep wood’s shadow. “Where?”

I opened the book again, not to the lost lament, but to the frontispiece. To the map we had scarcely glanced at in the cave’s frenzy. It was not a map of roads or towns. It was a map of memory. It charted hills not by their height, but by their old names. It showed rivers not by their flow, but by the songs they were said to sing. And in the north-west quadrant, drawn with a careful, delicate hand, was a symbol: a circle divided not by a line, but by a crack. Like a stone split by a single, deep root. The notation beside it, in the same rust-brown ink, read: *An Fhocal gan Briathar*. The Word without Speech.

“We go to the silence,” I said.

***

The forest taught us its grammar. We no longer moved through it; we moved with it. Elara led, but I began to see. I saw how the pine mast sloped in subtle watersheds, guiding water—and pursuers—along predictable channels. I saw how the spacing of the trunks could funnel sound or swallow it. This was not the Baron’s land, measured in acres and yields. This was the land of the book, measured in stories and sighs.

We walked against the grain.

It was a form of mutiny against the very logic of the hunt. We climbed where the terrain urged us to descend. We pressed into thickets of young spruce where the way was clawed and noisy, because Caden’s pincer would avoid such time-consuming snarls. We became inefficient, erratic, heretical in our pathfinding. The tension in my shoulders, a constant companion since the river, began to transmute. It was no longer the fear of the caught. It was the fierce, focused energy of the translator. I was translating the book’s hidden map into footfalls, its lost names into choices.

By late afternoon, the character of the wood changed. The pines gave way to great, gnarled oaks, their branches low and twisting, their leaves holding the green light of a earlier age. The air grew damp. The sound of water returned, not the roar of the river we had crossed, but a deep, resonant murmur, as if the earth itself were humming.

We found the source in a clearing that felt less discovered than remembered. A spring welled up from a cleft in a vast, moss-bearded outcrop of rock, pooling in a stone basin before slipping away as a slender stream. The rock face was not blank. It was carved.

The relief was worn, kissed smooth by centuries of weather and lichen. It showed two figures, not facing each other, but facing outward, their backs joined. One held something akin to a book. The other held a staff that was also a flowering branch. Their features were blurred into universality, but their posture spoke of a shared vigilance. Above them was the same symbol from the map: the circle divided by a crack.

*The Word without Speech.*

Elara approached the carved figures, her hand outstretched but not touching. “A sentinel post,” she breathed. “Not for an army. For knowledge.”

I understood. This was not a shrine to a god. It was a marker for a library. A place where the word was kept safe, not by being shouted from a tower, but by being folded into the land, remembered by the water and the stone. The spring was the librarian. The carving was the catalogue.

The awe from the cave returned, but purified, focused. This was the root Bernart’s line had hinted at. Not a chest in a hole, but a network in a living world. The cave had been an ark. This was a seed.

I knelt by the pool, the book heavy in my hands. This was the familiar setting charged with new meaning. The lectern was now a stone basin. The parchment was the living rock. My failure was no longer a private shame; it was a historical one. Could I, a product of towers and titles, of ink and isolation, learn to read this text?

I opened the book to the sodden lament. Then I did the thing that would have made my old master strike my hand. I lowered it, and I let the pages drink from the spring.

“What are you doing?” Elara’s voice was sharp.

“Giving it back its voice,” I said, and my own voice was strange to me.

The vellum darkened, the water spreading through the fibres in a blooming tide. The grey ink shimmered, seemed to float for a moment between dissolution and clarity. And there, on the ravaged page, the water performed a miracle of reconstruction. It connected the broken stems of letters, filled the blooms of damage with a translucent film that made the lost words appear as shadows beneath water. I could not read them directly. But I could see their shape, their rhythm on the line.

*Is e an t-ainm nach scriobhtar… ach a fhuaimear i sruth.*
“It is the name that is not written… but that is sounded in the stream.”

I had not resurrected the text. I had translated it into its true medium. It was not meant for the eye alone. It was meant for the ear, carried by water, remembered by stone. The memory was not in the ink. It was in the interaction. The book was not the truth; it was a key to the truth that lived here, in the humming earth, in the singing spring.

I began to speak the shadow-words aloud, my voice blending with the murmur of the water. They were not my words. They were not even words in a language I fully knew. They were sounds, gestures of breath and tongue that fit the cadence of the spring. I was not a scribe. I was a conduit.

Elara stood very still. The suspicion in her eyes melted into a dawning, fearful comprehension. This was the heresy that could not be burned. It was the identity that could not be scraped clean. Because it existed in the space between the thing and its echo, between the written and the remembered.

A branch snapped.

Not from the way we had come. From the north-east, the direction the carved sentinels faced.

We were not the quarry driven to a cliff. We had driven ourselves to the heart. And the hunt had refined its pattern once more.

Caden stepped from between two oaks. He was not a large man, but he gave the impression of density, as if he had absorbed the patience and silence of the trees. He wore greens and browns that seemed to shift with the dappled light. In his hands was a crossbow, not levelled, but held with a casual, absolute readiness. His eyes were the colour of flint, and they took in the clearing, the carving, the book in my wet hands, in one swift, comprehensive glance.

“The scribe,” he said. His voice was like the rustle of dry leaves. “And the guide.” He looked at Elara, and a flicker of something—regret, recognition—passed over his face. “Elara. I told the Baron your family’s loyalty would hold.”

“My family’s loyalty is to the land, Caden,” she said, her voice empty. “The Baron only rents it.”

He acknowledged this with a slight tilt of his head. His gaze returned to me, to the book. “That is the thing that should not be. You know the penalty for its possession. For its… activation.”

“It was already here,” I said. The sound of my voice, still resonant with the echo of the lament, surprised me. It was calm. “We only brought one piece back to where it belongs.”

“It belongs in the fire,” Caden said, and there was no malice in it, only a finality as plain as stone. “A quiet death. The world has moved on. These memories… they are a sickness. They divide loyalties. They make good servants question their masters. They make scribes forget their place.”

He raised the crossbow, not with a jerk, but with the smooth, inevitable motion of a tide coming in. The quarrel was tipped with grey steel. It was aimed at my heart, and at the book I held against it.

This was the limit. The purified test. The integration of all I had learned was not a sword-skill or a trick of stealth. It was a choice of texts. Before me was the text of power, written in steel and law, its grammar one of suppression and silence. Within me was the text of memory, written in water and stone, its grammar one of resonance and return. One sought to end the story. The other sought to change its telling.

I did not move to run. I did not plead.

I let the book fall open on the damp earth. I kept my eyes on Caden’s.

And I began to sing.

The sounds were the ones from the spring-water page, the shadow-words of the lament. They were not loud. They did not fill the clearing. They seeped into it. They married the murmur of the water, the sigh of the wind in the oak leaves. I sang the name that is not written, the name that is sounded in the stream. I was not Elara, with her knife and her fierce, practical love. I was the other sentinel, the one with the book. My weapon was the memory itself.

Caden’s finger tightened on the trigger. His knuckle whitened. And then it paused.

His flint eyes widened, not with fear, but with a profound, unsettling confusion. He was a man of patterns, and I had just broken the deepest one: the pattern of fear. The quarry should run. The heretic should beg or rage. The scribe should cower.

I sang. The ghost page against my chest felt warm.

A second figure emerged from the trees. Then a third. Huntsmen, armed with bows. They formed a half-circle behind Caden, their weapons ready. But they, too, hesitated, their discipline frayed by the strangeness of the scene: their master huntsman, frozen before a singing man and a wet book.

“Caden,” one of them muttered, a question and a warning.

Elara had not drawn her knife. She stood beside me, not as a shield, but as a witness. Her presence was a anchor in the reality of what was happening. This was real. The song was real.

Caden’s gaze broke from mine. It flickered to the carved sentinels on the rock, as if seeing them for the first time. He saw the symbol. The crack in the circle. His jaw tightened. He was a man of the land, too. He knew its older stories in his bones, even as he served the power that had buried them. The conflict was not in the clearing; it was in him. The heresy was not in the book; it was in the recognition of a pattern older than his Baron, older than any title.

“It is a sickness,” he repeated, but his voice had lost its certainty. It was a plea, now. To himself.

“No,” I said, letting the song fade into the natural sound of the place. “It is the land remembering its name. You are not hunting thieves. You are hunting a memory. And you cannot kill what is already woven into the water and the stone.”

For a long moment, there was only the sound of the spring and the breathing of armed men. The crossbow did not waver. It was the fulcrum on which two worlds balanced.

Then, with a sound that was part sigh, part snarl, Caden lowered the weapon. He did not un-cock it. He simply pointed it at the earth.

“Go,” he said, the word ripped from him. He did not look at his men. “Take your sickness north. Beyond the Black Ridge. If you are found in the Baron’s reach again, I will not stay my hand. The memory of the land will not stay the bolt.”

It was not mercy. It was a deeper form of exile. He was casting us out of the mapped world, into the blank spaces on his own charts. He was preserving the pattern of his loyalty by making us a part of a different one—the pattern of the forgotten.

I bent, slowly, and picked up the book. It was cooler now. The pages were already drying, the shadow-words faded once more into illegibility. The key had turned. The lock had opened, just for a moment.

We backed away, past the stone sentinels, into the deeper green of the oaks. The huntsmen watched us go, their faces unreadable masks. Caden stood before the spring, his head bowed, as if listening for the echo of a song he could no longer hear.

***

We walked until the twilight beneath the oaks became the absolute dark of night. No one followed. The hunt was over. We were no longer quarry. We were something else. Something expelled.

We did not stop until we found a hollow at the base of a great, lightning-blasted oak, its heartwood open to the sky like a wound. We crawled into the dry, fibrous darkness. The exhaustion was total, a collapse not of muscle, but of spirit. The confrontation was past. The cost was now to be counted.

Elara sat with her back to the inner wood, her knees drawn up. I could feel the tremor in her limbs where our shoulders touched. The bravery that had been a sustained note for days was fading into a shuddering silence.

“He knew me,” she said, her voice small in the dark. “He knew my father. My family has guided the Baron’s hunts for three generations.” She let out a breath that was almost a laugh. “We were the pattern.”

“And now?” I asked.

“Now I am a break in the pattern.” She turned her head toward me. I could barely see the outline of her face. “You sang. I have never heard… what was it?”

“The truth of the book,” I said. “It is not a thing to be kept. It is a thing to be performed. To be a scribe of this… you must become the page. You must let the memory speak, and in doing so, you erase yourself.”

The old self was gone. Shed completely. Not in a blaze, but in a slow substitution, like one language displacing another in the mind of a child. The man who had worried over the scrape of a parchment knife was dead. The man who remained was a vessel, hollowed out by fear and wonder, now filled with the echo of a spring and the shape of shadow-words. I was not a preserver. I was not an excavator. I was a site. The place where a forgotten thing could, for a moment, remember itself.

This was the transformation. It was not an acquisition of power, but a surrender of sovereignty. My identity was now a shared tenancy with a ghost. The battle for my mind was lost. I had let the older, more urgent story win. And in that loss, I found a terrible, fragile peace.

Elara’s hand found mine in the dark. Her fingers were cold. “What do we do now?”

I thought of the map in the book. The network of springs and stones, of sentinel posts and silent words. The Black Ridge was not the end. It was another boundary, another layer of forgetting. Beyond it lay the true margins, where the map became speculation, then legend, then blank vellum.

“We follow the silence,” I said. “We find the next word. And the next. We learn the whole grammar.”

“To what end?” Her question was not cynical. It was the necessary question of the guide, looking at an uncharted wilderness.

I had no answer that would fit in the world of Barons and bolts. But in the world of the book, the answer was clear.

“To remember the name,” I whispered. “So when the world’s sharpened steel finally comes for the last memory, it will find not a hidden scrap of vellum, but a living language. It will have to silence the water and level the stones. It will have to unmake the land itself.”

We slept, then, in the hollow of the dead tree. And for the first time since leaving the scriptorium, I did not dream of failing ink or accusing parchment. I dreamed of roots, deep and white and blind, feeling their way through the dark earth, connecting stone to spring, tree to tomb, in a vast, silent, remembering network.

***

Dawn came as a grey rumour through the broken roof of our shelter. We ate the last of our hard cheese and drank water from a rivulet that tasted of pine and peat. Our movements were slow, deliberate. The frantic energy of the hunt was gone. In its place was the steady, solemn purpose of pilgrims.

We climbed out of the hollow. The blasted oak stood on a rise, and from its base, looking north-west, we could see the land begin to change. The rolling forested hills grew steeper, more rugged. And on the far horizon, a wall of darker grey rose against the sky, sheer and forbidding: the Black Ridge. The end of the Baron’s reach. The beginning of the blank space.

But at our feet, almost hidden in the moss and ling, was a stone. It was not carved. It was simply a long, low slab of granite, half-sunk into the earth. Yet its placement felt intentional. I brushed away the damp moss from one end.

A single, faint mark was incised there. Not the cracked circle. This was different. A single, vertical line, crossed near the top by a shallow, angled dash. It could have been a bird in flight. Or a mark of direction.

It was a glyph from the book. One I had seen in the margin of the water-damaged lament. It meant, I now understood, *transition*. *A crossing over*.

Elara saw it and nodded. She shouldered her pack. “The next sentinel post is not a place. It’s a direction.”

We left the stone pointing our way. As we walked, the quality of the light changed, growing thinner, cooler. The oaks thinned and gave way to birch and hawthorn, their branches clutching at our clothes like beseeching fingers. The path, if it could be called that, was a deer track that wound upwards along the shoulder of a rising moor.

By midday, we had reached the tree line. Before us stretched a high, wind-scoured plateau of heather and rock, leading to the immense, brooding face of the Black Ridge. The sky was a vast bowl of fast-moving cloud. The wind had a voice here, a constant, low drone that spoke of exposure and age.

In the very centre of the plateau, perhaps half a mile distant, stood a single, twisted thorn tree, its shape a permanent scream against the wind. And from its branches, something fluttered. Not a leaf. Something pale and regular.

We exchanged a glance and moved toward it, our steps quickening despite our weariness.

As we neared, the fluttering material resolved itself. Parchment. Three sheets, pinned to the thorn’s branches by sharp slivers of black stone. They flapped and strained in the relentless wind.

My heart clenched. This was not ancient. This was new.

I reached the tree, Elara a step behind, her hand on her knife. I carefully loosened one of the stone pins and took down the first sheet.

The hand was swift, educated, but not a scribe’s. It was a scholar’s hand, precise and without flourish. The language was the common tongue of the realm, but the words were chilling.

*To the Scribe of the Unwritten Name,*

*Your performance at the Sentinel Spring has been noted. Caden is a blunt instrument, useful for culling deer and dissent. His failure is predictable. The memory you carry is of interest to a higher mind.*

*You seek a grammar. I have compiled the dictionary. The scattered words you cherish are but fragments of a Lexicon Mortis, the Language of the Earth’s Memory. It is a language not of naming, but of un-naming. It does not describe the world; it argues with its present state. A dangerous, seductive heresy.*

*The Baron hunts a ghost. I hunt the ghost’s breath. Your book is a primer. I possess the Index.*

*Come north. Pass the Black Ridge at the Dragon’s Tooth canyon. There is a settlement in the valley beyond, a place of those who remember differently. Ask for the Collector.*

*Bring the book. In exchange, I offer the context for its ruin. You wish to give the buried identity a voice? I can teach you the tongue it speaks. You wish to retain your own story? That, I fear, is a currency no longer accepted here.*

*Choose silence, and you will wander these margins until the wind scours the words from your skin and the ghosts from your mind. Choose knowledge, and you will understand the price of the memory you carry. It is a price paid not in blood, but in self.*

*I await your decision at the edge of the map.*

*—The Collector*

The wind moaned through the thorn tree. The other two pages were copies of the same message, placed so no approach could miss it.

Elara took the parchment from my numb fingers, her eyes scanning the lines. “A trap,” she said flatly.

“Is it?” I asked. The voice in the letter was not Caden’s. It was not the Baron’s. It was a voice from a different kind of darkness—one that understood the shape of the void I carried. It knew the term *Lexicon Mortis*. It spoke of the ghost’s breath.

The Collector was the antagonist I had been waiting for. Not one of steel, but of knowledge. One who did not seek to destroy the memory, but to own it, to catalogue it. To strip it of its danger by understanding it completely. His was the final, purified test. To face not ignorance, but a twisted, mirror-image scholarship.

The central conflict was resolved with Caden. The world of law and suppression had cast us out. But a new conflict crystallized in that moment, colder and more intimate. The conflict over the soul of the memory itself. Would it be a living language of the land, or a specimen in a collector’s cabinet? Would its voice be a song in a spring, or an entry in an index?

I looked from the fluttering pages to the immense, dark wall of the Ridge. The Dragon’s Tooth canyon was a visible notch in its forbidding face, a deep shadow cleaving the rock.

The hook was set. Not in my flesh, but in the very core of my purpose. The Collector awaited. He offered the very thing I had sacrificed everything for: understanding. And he demanded the one thing I had left: the last shred of agency over the story now living within me.

I took the book from my pack. It was cool, its damp pages now warped and stiff. A primer. He had called it a primer.

I looked at Elara. Her face was set, resigned. She saw the choice in my eyes.

“The edge of the map,” I said, my voice barely audible over the wind.

She nodded once, a sharp, final motion. “Then we go off it.”

We turned our faces toward the notch in the Ridge, toward the canyon with the dragon’s name, leaving the fluttering parchment to its endless, whispering conversation with the wind. The confrontation with the world was over. The conversation with the void was just beginning.



---

# Chapter 9

## **Chapter 9**

The memory was a country, and I was its sole, unwilling citizen.

It lived behind my eyes, a landscape painted in the pigments of absence. The scent of wet stone from the canyon, the taste of iron on the wind, the specific chill of a shadow that had never known sun—these were not recollections. They were territories. I walked them in my sleep. I governed them in my waking hours. The Lexicon Mortis was not a list of words; it was a cartography of the void, and the map was drawn on the parchment of my nerves.

We came down from the high, wind-scoured shoulders of the Ridge into the valley they called the Tarnished Cup. It was the ordinary world, or what passed for it now. A place of mud and stubborn green, of stone fences holding back the press of the wild, of hearth-smoke that smelled of peat and damp wool. It was a world that had forgotten the taste of the ghost’s breath. To me, it seemed unbearably loud, a cacophony of solid, simple things.

Elara walked beside me, a pace to my left and half a step behind. Her silence was different. It was not the loaded silence of the memory-haunted, but the clean, sharp silence of a blade returned to its sheath. The confrontation with Caden, with the Baron’s law, was a finished line. She had turned the page. My page was stuck, the ink smearing, the same phrase written over and over: *He knows the shape of the void.*

“The primer,” I said. The word felt strange in my mouth, too scholarly for this muddy track. “He called it a primer.”

Elara glanced at me, her eyes scanning the tree line from habit. “A child’s first book. Letters and pictures.”

“Yes. But for what language?” I hefted the damp, warped book in my pack. It was no longer just a book. It was a keyhole. And the Collector held the key. “It’s not a translation. It’s… an induction. A way to teach the mind to perceive the grammar of absence.”

She absorbed this, her boots sinking into the soft earth. “So you carry the lesson. And he is the master.”

“He wants to own the syllabus.” The metaphor was apt. This was the reward, the twisted elixir I had brought back from the canyon’s edge: not a cure, but a curriculum. A way to systematize the haunting. To make the unspeakable into a subject for study. The thought was a cold fever in my veins, equal parts revulsion and a terrible, gravitational pull.

***

The village of Brighthollow clung to the banks of a sullen, slow-moving stream. Its houses were low and grey, their thatch patched with moss. It recognized us, but not as we were.

Old Man Hestor was the first. He was mending a wattle fence by the common well, his hands like knotted roots. He saw us emerge from the treeline and straightened, a slow, painful unbending of a lifetime of bending to the soil. His eyes, milky with years, passed over Elara’s sword, over my pack with its strange, angular bulge, and settled on our faces.

“You’re back from the Tooth,” he stated. His voice was the sound of gravel shifting. It was not a question. In places like Brighthollow, the Ridge was a clock, and the Dragon’s Tooth its black, marking hour. To go toward it was one kind of folly. To return from it was another.

“We are,” Elara said, her voice neutral, a soldier reporting a change in watch.

He nodded, once. A deep, tectonic motion. “The spring at the Croft’s end. It went sour two days past. Water tastes of rust and bad dreams. The widow’s boy won’t stop shaking.” His gaze shifted to me, and in his clouded eyes I saw a flicker of the old knowledge, the kind that doesn’t live in books. “You have the look of a man who’s drunk from a stranger well.”

I felt the memory-country shift inside me. A sour spring. Water tasting of rust and bad dreams. It was a dialect. A crude, village idiom for the ghost’s breath.

“I have,” I said, the admission leaving my lips like a confession to a secular priest.

He grunted, as if I’d confirmed the price of eggs. “See to it, then.”

It was not a welcome. It was a sentencing. A recognition, yes, but of a specific and alien utility. I was not the man who had left, chasing parchment phantoms. I was the man who had returned, carrying the phantom in his bones. I was a tool for a specific task. The world did not celebrate my journey; it presented me with its bill.

***

The widow’s croft was at the village’s frayed edge, where the cultivated land gave way to marsh and scrub. The house sagged under its own weight of grief and damp. The boy, perhaps ten years old, lay on a pallet by the cold hearth. His skin was the colour of old wax, and a fine, constant tremor ran through him, like a mouse was trapped under his skin. His eyes were open, fixed on the smoke-blackened rafters, seeing nothing of this world.

The spring was a stone-lined hollow behind the house. The water should have been clear, welling up from some clean, secret aquifer. Instead, it was the colour of weak tea, and a faint, coppery smell hung above it—the smell of blood and wet metal. The smell of the canyon.

Elara stood guard at the gate, a statue of watchfulness. The widow, a woman whose face had been worn smooth by worry, wrung her hands in her apron. “He just went to draw water for the goats,” she whispered, as if the spring itself might hear. “Came back like this. Talking in words that weren’t his. Words that… hurt the ears.”

I knelt by the spring’s edge. I did not need to taste it. I could *feel* it. A thin, sickly tendril of the same absence that lived in me. It was a leak. A seepage from the memory-country into the water table of the real. The Collector would have a name for this phenomenon. He would have a jar to put it in.

But I was not the Collector.

I opened my pack and took out the primer. Its damp pages were stiff, the warped vellum like a blister. I did not open it. I laid my hands upon its cool cover. The elixir of my quest was not a liquid in a vial. It was this: a heightened, painful sensitivity. A tuning of the self to the frequency of loss.

I closed my eyes. Let the ordinary sounds fade—the widow’s breathing, the distant caw of a crow, the sigh of the wind in the sedge. In the darkness behind my lids, I felt for the memory. Not the Lexicon, not the grand, terrible architecture of it, but the specific, localized ache of this place. The ghost’s breath was not uniform. Here, by this sad little spring, it carried the particular despair of a creature trapped in mud, its final, bubbling exhalation lost for centuries.

My own sorrow, the vast and personal one, was a different key. But it was made of the same stuff. I could resonate with it.

I began to hum. Not a song, not a *canso* of courtly love, but a low, wordless drone from the back of my throat. It was the sound a stone might make if it could mourn its own erosion. I pushed my awareness, that terrible new sense, down into the fouled water, not to fight the memory, but to meet it. To acknowledge its shape. To sing back its own silent, stranded note.

In my mind, I formed the concept, not in the Collector’s clinical terms, but in the only language that fit: *I see your ending. It is here, in this mud. It is over.*

The humming vibration in my throat seemed to sink into the earth. For a long moment, nothing. Then, a shudder passed through the water. The coppery film on the surface trembled and began to contract, drawing in on itself like a dying ripple. The colour cleared, from tea to a hesitant transparency. The metallic stench broke apart, carried off on the breeze, leaving only the clean, cold smell of deep earth.

From the house, a sharp, gasping cry. Then a wail. The widow’s cry, but it was a sound of relief, of shock. Elara’s posture changed, a slight easing of the shoulders.

I opened my eyes. The spring was just a spring. The leak was patched. Not by force, but by a kind of empathetic completion. I had used the elixir—the knowledge, the attunement—to heal a small, broken piece of the world. I had shared the boon, and it had cost me a fresh layer of my own peace. The memory-country now contained a new province: a boggy death in Brighthollow.

I stood, my knees cracking. The primer under my hands felt inert again. Just a book. For now.

The widow rushed out, tears cutting tracks through the dust on her face. “He’s awake! He’s asking for bread! The shaking… it’s stopped.” She looked at me, and her eyes were no longer fearful, but wide with a kind of terrified awe. “What are you?”

I had no answer that would fit in her world. I was the scribe of the unscribable. The singer of the silent song. I was healed of my ignorance, and sick with its cure.

“A passerby,” I said, my voice rough from the humming. “The water should be sweet now.”

***

That night, we slept in Hestor’s byre, the sweet, dry smell of hay a balm after the marsh. The village had no inn, and we were not offered a hearth. Our new place was here, in the margins, with the animals and the stored harvest. It was fitting.

Elara sharpened her dagger by the light of a single tallow dip. The rhythmic *shush-shush* of stone on steel was a grounding, ordinary music. “You gave him back his son,” she said, not looking up.

“I gave him back a boy who will dream of drowning in mud for the rest of his life,” I replied, staring at the thatch above. “I traded one poison for another. A quieter one.”

“It was a kindness.”
“Was it?” I turned my head to look at her. “The Collector would say I applied a folk remedy to a metaphysical infection. That I treated a symptom and left the disease to find another vent. He would be right.”

“The Collector,” she said, the word flat. “He lives in a world of perfect understanding. We live in this one. Where sometimes a quieter poison is the only medicine you have.”

She had found her new place with the swift, sure adaptation of a soldier. Her purpose was clear: guard the vessel. The vessel being me, and the terrible knowledge I carried. Her world had shrunk to a single, clean duty. Mine had expanded into a chaotic, echoing empire of the dead.

I envied her.

My new place was nowhere and everywhere. I was a citizen of memory, a diplomat to the void. I could walk through Brighthollow and feel the latent sorrow in the stones of its foundations, the minor-key hum of every small, forgotten loss in its history. The elixir had opened a channel I could not close. The world was no longer solid. It was a palimpsest, and I could read every layer of its pain.

This was my reward. This permanent, piercing clarity. I was recognized, not as a hero, but as a strange and useful phenomenon, like a water-diviner or a lightning-rod. I was healed of the simple desire to forget, and in its place was the colossal burden of remembering *for* everyone else.

And yet.

Lying there in the hay, listening to the calm, mortal sounds of cattle and Elara’s honing stone, I felt a sliver of something that was not despair. It was responsibility. The primer was a keyhole, and the Collector held the key. But I held the door. The memory was in *me*. It was my country. My jurisdiction. He wanted to catalogue its flora and fauna, to pin its spectres to a board. But I was its land, its weather, its law.

The hook that evening was not set in the outer world. It was an internal pivot, a hardening of a choice I did not know I had made until that moment.

I would go to the Collector. I would take his understanding, his maps of the void. I would use his scholarship to learn the boundaries and behaviours of the thing inside me.

But I would not let him own it. I would not let him turn the song into a specimen. The Lexicon Mortis was a living language, and I was its last, flawed speaker. My new place was as its guardian. Not to bury it, and not to display it, but to bear it, faithfully and terribly, as the land bears the weight of its own history.

The conversation with the void was just beginning. And I was no longer just a scribe taking dictation. I was preparing to negotiate terms.

In the dark of the byre, with the smell of hay and animal, I began, very quietly, to compose the first words of my reply.



---

# Chapter 10

## **Chapter 10**

The world was a palimpsest, and I was its reluctant archivist.

I walked through the lower cloister of Brighthollow, and the stones sang a low, discordant threnody. It was not a sound, but a pressure—the accumulated residue of seven centuries of quiet grief: a novice’s homesickness etched into a flagstone, a master’s despair over a failed experiment bleeding from the mortar, the phantom ache of a sprained ankle from a long-dead stable boy. The elixir’s work was complete. The channel was open, the static of human sorrow a permanent, whispering backdrop to every step. I had thought it a burden. Now, I was learning to walk under its weight. It was the climate of my new country.

Elara found me by the well in the herb garden. She did not approach like one approaches a friend, but like a scout assessing a changed landmark. Her hands, usually busy with strap or steel, were still at her sides.

“You’re standing differently,” she said. No greeting. An observation, filed.

“The ground is louder,” I replied. The memory of the well was of a child, Aelis, who had dropped a silver button here sixty years ago and wept for a week. The damp, cool sadness of it seeped up through the soles of my boots. “It requires a different balance.”

She nodded, as if I’d confirmed a suspicion. “They want you in the scriptorium. Brother Anselm has a commission. A land grant for the Lord Seneschal.”

A mundane task. A test. I was no longer just the infirm scribe, the convalescent curiosity. I was the lexicon. The phenomenon. They needed to see if I could still function as a tool.

“And you?” I asked. “What do you want?”

She picked up a smooth river stone from the edge of the well, turned it over in her palm. Her own memories of this place were recent, sharp: the frustration of waiting for me to heal, the grim satisfaction of whetting a blade to a killing edge, the complex, unspoken relief when I awoke changed. I could feel the edges of those thoughts, not their content, but their emotional shape, like heat radiating from a banked fire.

“I want to know if you’re still in there,” she said, her voice low. “Or if you’re just a… repository.”

I met her gaze. The directness was a lifeline. “The repository has opinions. It remembers you dragging it through a fen. It remembers your promise to leave it for dead if it became a danger.” I allowed a faint, dry echo of our old dynamic into my tone. “It found that refreshingly honest.”

A flicker at the corner of her mouth. Not a smile, but an acknowledgment. The tension in her shoulders eased a fraction. “Good. Honesty is all we have left. The Collector’s man is here. He arrived at dawn.”

The hook was set. The internal pivot I had made in the byre met its external counterpart. “Where?”

“The guest house. He looks like a tax assessor. He carries a ledger.”

Of course. The Collector would not come himself. He would send a clerk, a procurer of rare texts. I was simply a living manuscript to be appraised.

“Will you come with me?” I asked.

“To see the taxman? It’s the most exciting thing that’s happened all week.” She tossed the river stone back. It landed with a definitive *clack* that silenced, for a moment, the whispering memory of the lost button.

***

The scriptorium was a symphony of absence.

I took my old place at the oak lectern. The familiar scents—dust, vellum, iron gall, beeswax—were now underscored by a hundred other histories. The faint, greasy despair of a monk who could never get his minims straight on this very desk. The bright, brittle pride of another who had illuminated a capital so beautifully it made his hands ache for years after. The parchment on the lectern was fresh, clean, a blank potential. But the air around it was thick with the ghosts of every word ever written in this room.

Brother Anselm hovered, a nervous sparrow. “The usual hand, if you please, Alaric. A clear, formal charter. The boundaries are listed here.” He placed a scrap of notes beside me, his fingers twitching away as if from a hot stove. He could not meet my eyes for long. I was a walking reliquary of the death-song, and my presence made the sanctity of his scriptorium feel provisional.

I dipped the pen. The ink was black and perfect. I began the formula. *Sciant presentes et futuri…*

My hand moved, steady and practiced. But I was not merely transcribing. As I formed each letter, I felt the memory embedded in the act itself. The thousand times I had made that particular loop of the ‘*e*’, the muscle-memory of patience and suppression. Now, I did not suppress. I let the memory of the act flow through me, acknowledging its depth, its repetition. The text emerged not just as ink on skin, but as a layer atop a thousand other layers of striving and silence. I was not writing a land grant. I was adding a single, deliberate line to the vast, collective manuscript of this place.

Anselm peered over my shoulder as I finished, his breath shallow. He was looking for tremors, for signs of possession, for the jagged script of the Lexicon Mortis to break through the orderly Carolingian minuscule.

He found only flawless penmanship.

“Adequate,” he murmured, disappointment and relief warring in his voice. He had hoped, perhaps, for a spectacle. Or feared one. “You may… you may go.”

I placed the pen down precisely. “Thank you, Brother.”

As I left, I let my fingers brush the doorjamb. A shock of sensation—broader, deeper than the minor griefs of the room. This was older. A foundational sorrow. The memory of the master mason who had set this stone, hearing of his son’s death at sea the very week this wing was completed. His profound, wordless grief was sealed into the cornerstone of Brighthollow itself, a bass note beneath all the others. I absorbed it, recognized it, and moved on. I was learning to navigate the strata.

***

The Collector’s man was, as Elara had said, a clerk. His name was Peltast. He was a narrow man in a narrow robe of grey wool, with a face like a folded letter. The guest chamber smelled of him: dry paper, faint vinegar, and the peculiar, empty scent of a mind that sought to organize, not to feel.

He did not rise when I entered. Elara leaned against the wall by the door, a silent sentinel.

“Alaric of Brighthollow,” Peltast stated. He opened his ledger. “Subject of the Brighthollow Incident. Survivor. Primary vessel for the untitled mnemonic contagion, colloquially ‘the death-song.’” He looked up. His eyes were the colour of dust. “My principal wishes to acquire your testimony.”

“I am not a book to be purchased,” I said, my voice calm in the humming room. The chamber’s memories were simple: boredom, impatience, transient sleep.

“Everything is a text,” Peltast replied, unblinking. “A battle is a text written in blood and terrain. A life is a text written in actions and decay. You contain a unique text. My principal collects, collates, and preserves. He offers understanding in exchange for access.”

“Understanding,” I echoed. “Or dissection?”

“The two are often synonymous for the rare specimen.” He said it without malice. It was a fact of his world. “You are anomalous. A stable host. You have not gone mad, nor have you expired. You have… integrated the text. My principal’s maps of the void could teach you its grammar. You could learn to read what you bear, not just hear it.”

It was the offer I had expected. The bait I had decided to take. But hearing it in his bloodless voice solidified my resolve. He spoke of the song as a dead language. I knew it to be a living one.

“I will come,” I said. Peltast’s quirked eyebrow was his only sign of surprise. “I will grant access to my… testimony. But my terms are these: I am the scribe, not the page. I will not be bled, hypnotized, or physically altered in any search for the source. The memory resides in me. Any examination is conducted with my consent, through my interpretation. I am the sole translator.”

Peltast’s pen scratched in the ledger. “Unprecedented.”

“So is the text,” Elara said from the doorway, her voice a soft rasp of steel on stone.

The clerk considered. “My principal’s scholarship is predicated on direct observation of the phenomenon.”

“And I *am* the phenomenon,” I said, leaning forward slightly. The movement stirred the air, and with it, the deeper memory of the room—a sudden, sharp recollection of a traveller who had received news of a plague in his home city here, in this chair. The panic, the gut-deep dread. I let it wash through me, and did not let it touch my face. “You may observe *me*. You do not get to peel me apart to see how I work. These are my terms. The song is my country. You are a visiting scholar. You abide by my laws.”

There was a long silence filled only with the scratch of Peltast’s pen as he noted my conditions. Finally, he looked up. “I will convey your position. My principal is a rationalist. He may accept the limitations if the access is sufficiently valuable. We depart in three days.”

He said nothing more, dismissing us by returning his full attention to his ledger. The audience was over.

Outside, in the sun-dappled courtyard, Elara let out a slow breath. “You sounded like a king laying down edicts. A king of a very strange, very small country.”

“It is the smallest country,” I said, touching my chest. “And the strangest. And its only citizen is currently terrified.”

“But you’re going.”
“I am. I need his maps. But I will not let him draw my borders.”

***

The lingering thread surfaced that evening, as threads do, in the quietest hour.

Old Finn, the groundskeeper, found me at the vespers bell. His face, a landscape of wrinkles and kindness, was etched with a new worry. “It’s the ash tree,” he said, wringing his cloth cap. “The great one by the eastern wall. It’s sickening. Dying from the top down. Brother Anselm says it must be felled, before it drops a limb on the wall.” His eyes, milky with age, held a deeper distress. “It’s her tree, lad. You remember?”

I did. Not just from my own childhood, but from the memory in the very soil. Aelis. The girl with the silver button. She had planted the ash as a sapling, a secret act of rebellion and hope on her tenth saint’s day. She had tended it, whispered to it. Its roots had grown down through the strata of her long, quiet life as a lay-sister, its branches a testament to her steadfast, unnoticed joy. The tree was not just a tree. It was the living monument of a specific, gentle soul. To the others, it was a hazard. To Finn, who had been a boy when she was an old woman, it was a tombstone.

“Show me,” I said.

The great ash stood silver in the twilight, but its crown was indeed skeletal, black against the deepening blue. Anselm’s practical eye was correct. It was dying. I placed a hand on its ridged bark.

The memory that flooded me was not Aelis’s. It was the tree’s own. Or rather, the memory of the land of the tree’s life. I felt the decades of sun and storm, the deep, slow pull of water through its roots, the crackle of frost in its sapwood. And I felt the sickness—a subtle, creeping wrongness, a metallic whisper in the groundwater that fed it. It was not a blight or a beetle. It was a poison. A memory of poison.

I knelt, digging my fingers into the damp earth at the base. The soil here remembered everything. It remembered the sweet decay of autumn leaves, the slow journey of worms. And it remembered, two winters past, a cracked ceramic jar, emptied hastily behind the herb shed. The contents—a toxic residue from one of the alchemy chamber’s failed experiments—had seeped down, down, a slow, venomous trickle finding the deepest thirst of the ash’s roots.

“It’s not sick,” I said, my voice rough with the taste of iron and rue on my tongue. “It’s been poisoned. By accident. The waste from Brother Hewitt’s trial with vitriol.”

Finn stared. “How could you know that?”

“The land remembers.” I stood, wiping my hand on my tunic. The knowledge was clear, a stark picture in the dark album of the ground. “The tree is a casualty. It doesn’t need to be felled. The source needs to be dug up. The soil here must be removed, down to three feet, and replaced with clean earth from the western wood. The tree may yet recover.”

It was a gardener’s solution. A practical, physical fix. But it had come not from horticultural knowledge, but from listening to the memory of the poison itself.
