Now, I could begin.

I dipped the pen anew. I set the nib to the skin, below the wandering vine. I did not think of words. I thought of the rhythm. *Thump-scrape.* The pressure, the release. *Hiss-ting.* The glide, the stop.

My hand moved.

*Lo solelh de mon pais es remas*
*En cimal de la rocha, en l’ombra del pas.*

The sun of my country has remained
On the mountain’s crest, in the shadow of the pass.

The letters formed. They were my own hand, but heavier, less florid. They were the hand of a man who had held a plough, not a pen. The ink was dark and sure. There was no hesitation. The words were not invented. They were excavated. They were there, waiting in the strata.

I wrote of the light. Not the general light, but the specific, honey-thick light of a late afternoon in early October, when the dust of the threshing floor hangs in the air, turning every beam into a solid thing. I wrote of the sound of the stream behind our house, not as music, but as a constant, white noise of time passing, against which all other sounds were measured. I wrote of the taste of the bread my mother baked, not with sentiment, but with the precise vocabulary of hunger and satisfaction—the crust that shattered, the crumb that clung to the roof of the mouth, the faint bitterness of the rye.

I did not write “I missed.” I wrote “The gate hinge had a particular cry.” I did not write “I loved.” I wrote “The stone of the hearth held the summer’s warmth until midnight.”

It was a song of details. A census of a lost world. Each line was a relic, held up to the light of this new, dark room. And as I wrote, something happened. The chorus in the room began to dissolve. Not to leave, but to merge. The essence of my mother’s turning hands flowed into the description of the bread. The essence of my father’s braced shoulders became the line about the load of firewood that never seemed to diminish, no matter how much you burned. They were no longer watching presences. They were the ink. They were the very fiber of the vellum.

I wrote for hours. The candle died. The grey light of dawn seeped into the room, colourless and clean. I lit no new flame. The ink was black enough to see.

I reached the bottom of the page. My hand ached, a deep, good ache. The page was full. Not with a finished song, but with the first movement of one. It was a foundation.

I set the pen down. The silence returned, but it was different. It was not a waiting silence. It was a silence of completion. Of a thing now existing in the world.

I looked at the sheet. The black vine at the top, my accidental illumination. The solid blocks of text below. It was not beautiful. It was true. It was a map of a territory that no longer existed, drawn by its only surviving native.

And I understood the consequence. This was not a private meditation. This was a document. To have written it was to have issued a challenge. A challenge to the silence that had enveloped that life. A challenge to the world that had taken it for granted, that had ground it into flour and asked for more bread.

This parchment was evidence.

Someone would read it. Master Thomas, perhaps, if I ever showed it to him. Or another scribe, going through my things after I was dead. Or a stranger, finding it in a market stall, bundled with other waste paper. It would be read. And in the reading, the lost country would, for a moment, exist again in a mind not my own. It would be translated, misunderstood, perhaps cherished. It would live.

That was the transformation. Not in me, but in the memory itself. It was no longer a passive, aching thing inside my skull. It was an active, mobile thing in the world. I had poured it from the crucible of myself, and in the pouring, it had changed state. From feeling to fact.

The hook was set. The line was taut. I had begun the reeling in.

But a fisherman knows the moment the hook goes into the fish’s mouth is not the end of the struggle. It is the beginning of a new, more dangerous phase. The fish feels the steel. It understands the constraint. It will fight. It will dive for the deep, dark places, and it will take all your strength, all your line, all your skill to bring it to the light.

My song was the hook. My lost country was the fish. And the deep, dark water it would now try to flee into was not the past. It was the present. It was the city around me, with its own hungry claims, its own towers of power, its own scribes who wrote not to remember, but to record debts, to levy taxes, to condemn.

I had written a truth. Truths have enemies. They disrupt the convenient fictions by which men live. The fiction that the past is dead. The fiction that the peasant’s life has no interior, no poetry, no sun caught on a mountain crest.

I rolled the parchment carefully. I did not tie it. I let it rest, curled in on itself, a sleeping serpent of ink and memory.

The dawn was full now. Sounds rose from the street: a cart’s rumble, a shouted greeting, the clang of a shutter being opened. The world of tasks and errands was awakening.

I had my own errand. I had promised Master Thomas a fair copy of a mercantile contract by noon. The world of debt and duty had not paused for my night of translation.

I stood. My body was stiff, cold. I was a vessel that had poured itself out, and now stood empty, waiting to be filled again with the mundane. But I was not the same vessel. The crucible had left its mark. The shape was altered.

As I gathered my tools for the day’s work—the mundane pens, the common ink, the coarse paper for drafts—I glanced at the rolled vellum on the desk. It seemed to hum with a low, persistent frequency, like a bell struck once and still vibrating.

The reeling in had begun. The line was cutting through the water. I could feel the first, powerful tremor of resistance.

The fish had felt the hook. And far below, in the depths I had just charted, it was turning.



---

# Chapter 16

## **Chapter 16**

The contract was a cage of words, and I was its keeper.

It lay on the worktable in Master Thomas’s counting-house, a sprawl of draft and redraft on cheap, grey paper. The air here smelled of dust, of ledger-mould, of the faint, sour sweat of anxiety that seeped from the merchants who came to have their agreements fixed in ink. No beeswax here. No ghost of meadows. Only the acrid bite of common oak-gall ink, thickened with too much gum, and the damp wool of the clerk who sat opposite me, his quill scratching out columns of figures like a beetle traversing a leaf.

My task was simple. To take the agreed terms between Thomas, representing a consortium of Lombard investors, and a Florentine wool-factor, and render them into a Latin so precise, so legally hermetic, that not a single ducat could escape its clauses. *In nomine Domini, amen. Pateat universis…* Let it be known to all. It was a language of closure. A language of walls.

My own hand moved across the paper, steady, professional. It was a different hand from the one that had traced the *canso* in the dark hours. That hand had been a conduit, trembling with the pressure of a voice passing through it. This hand was a mason, laying each word like a fitted stone, mortar of syntax between them. I felt the division within me, a crack running from crown to heel. One half here, in the chill light of commerce. The other half still vibrating, tuned to the hum of the rolled vellum in my room.

The clerk, a man named Alard, blew on a column of sums to dry them. “You are quiet today, Estève.”

“The work requires quiet,” I said, not looking up.

“It requires a head for numbers, not for silence. You seem elsewhere.” His tone was not unkind, merely observational. He was a man who lived in the perpetual present of debits and credits. The past was a column to be reconciled and closed; the future, one to be projected. He had no sense of the deep water.

“I am here,” I said, and the statement felt like a lie. I was and was not. The part of me that was here was a shell, performing a learned skill. The essential part—the part that remembered, that sang back the shape of a lost mountain against the sky—was coiled around the memory of the night’s work, feeling the tremor on the line.

I finished a clause regarding interest for late payment. The words were dry husks. *Si vero in dicto termino solutio non facta fuerit…* If indeed payment shall not have been made at the stated term… They carried no scent, no texture. They were coins rubbed smooth of their imprint.

Master Thomas entered, a ledger under his arm. His gaze swept the room, settling on our table. He came over, a compact man whose energy seemed to vibrate just beneath his skin, like a plucked wire. He did not look at my face, but at the growing text on the page before me.

“The Florentine returns at the second hour after noon,” he said. “It must be ready for sealing. The witnesses are arranged.”

“It will be ready,” I said.

His eyes flicked to me, then. They were the colour of slate, and just as absorbent. “Your work is always ready. It is your manner that is sometimes… delayed.” He leaned a fraction closer, his voice dropping, though Alard, engrossed in his figures, paid no heed. “The mind is a ledger, Estève. Credit and debit. A man who spends too long in contemplation of a single, past entry risks insolvency in the present. The page must turn.”

He was not speaking of the contract. He had not asked about my night, but he had sensed the expenditure. He was a man who measured everything, even the invisible currency of attention. His warning was a professional courtesy, and a threat. I was a useful instrument. Instruments must be ready to hand, their edge maintained. An instrument that became lost in its own resonance was a risk.

“The page turns, Master Thomas,” I said, meeting his gaze. “I am turning it.”

He held my look for a moment longer, then gave a short, sharp nod. “See that you do. The Florentine is impatient, and impatience is the enemy of good terms.” He moved away, his attention already leaping to the next item, the next calculation.

I returned to the cage of words. But his metaphor lingered. *The mind is a ledger.* For him, it was. For me, it was a landscape. A country. And last night, I had not merely reviewed an entry; I had repatriated a province. I had sung its anthem. Such an act does not appear as a balanced column. It appears as a seismic event, a redrawn border. The ground under my feet, here in this room of dust and debt, was no longer the same ground.

I completed the fair copy by midday. The ink was black, uniform, impeccable. I sanded it, blew the grit away, rolled it with a strip of parchment for binding. It was a perfect thing. A closed loop. I presented it to Master Thomas. He scanned it with the rapid, hungry eyes of a hawk, seeking the flaw, the loophole, the soft place. He found none.

“Good,” he said, the word a token of value. “Now, the inventory for the Bordeaux shipment. Alard has the particulars.”

The work was a river, and it flowed on. I moved from the contract to the inventory, a list of tuns of wine, of sacks of woad, of bolts of Flemish cloth. Each item a tangible thing, its value fixed, its destination known. My pen recorded their passage from one ownership to another. This was the language of the present city: a language of possession, of movement, of surface. It wrote upon the world, but it did not listen to the world’s echoes.

As the light began to slant, thick and honeyed with afternoon dust, a different kind of messenger arrived.

He was a young man, perhaps fifteen, dressed in the livery of a great household—a tunic of murrey and green. He stood in the doorway of the counting-house, uncertain amidst the tables and scribes.

“I seek Estève the scribe,” he announced, his voice cracking slightly on the last word.

All scratching stopped. A dozen clerks looked up, then looked at me. Master Thomas, from his desk by the strongbox, lifted his head like a hound catching a scent.

I stood. “I am he.”

The boy approached, holding out a small, sealed billet. The wax was a deep crimson, unmarked by any signet ring. “For you, sir.”

I took it. The paper was fine, linen-rag, not the coarse stuff of my drafts. The silence in the room was a palpable thing, a held breath. Messengers from liveried servants did not come for contract scribes. I broke the seal. The hand within was a swift, educated cursive, not one I recognized.

*The light you cast is of a particular quality. One who appreciates such illumination would speak with you. Come to the house with the green door in the Rue des Jardins at the ringing of the Compline bell. Ask for Sebastian.*

There was no signature. No title. Only the words, and the faint, elusive scent of orris root clinging to the paper.

I felt the eyes of the room upon me. Master Thomas’s gaze was a weight. I folded the paper, slipped it into the pouch at my belt.

“Thank you,” I said to the boy, my voice carefully flat. “There is no reply.”

The boy bowed, turned, and was gone. The scratching of pens resumed, but the rhythm was different now, punctuated by glances, by the silent communication of raised eyebrows. I sat back down to the Bordeaux inventory. The numbers blurred before my eyes. *The light you cast.* It could only refer to one thing. The vellum. The song. The hook had not only been felt by the fish. It had been seen. Its gleam had attracted a different kind of attention.

Compline. The last canonical hour of the day. When the city settled into watchful darkness, and honest men barred their doors.

Master Thomas appeared at my elbow. He did not look at me, but at the half-finished inventory. “A personal matter, Estève?”

“It would seem so, Master.”

“Personal matters have a way of becoming professional liabilities.” He picked up a finished sheet, pretended to examine it. “The house with the green door in the Rue des Jardins. That is near the Bishop’s palace. A neighbourhood of… subtle influences.” He put the sheet down. “A scribe’s value lies in his neutrality. His hand must be a clear pane of glass, through which the intentions of others are seen without distortion. A pane that reflects its own light is soon broken.”

“I understand,” I said.

“Do you?” He finally looked at me. The slate-coloured eyes were not angry, but calculating. “You are an asset. I do not wish to see an asset compromised. Finish your work. Go to your appointment. But remember: you are a scribe. You record. You do not originate. You translate. You do not compose. The moment you believe otherwise, you become something else. And something else has a different market value. Often a lower one.”

He walked away, leaving the chill of his assessment in the air. He was not wrong. In his world, he was profoundly right. I was a tool. Tools do not have histories. They do not sing songs of sorrow. They have edges, and purposes, and a price.

I finished the inventory as the bells rang for Vespers. The mundane world had reclaimed its due. I cleaned my pens, stoppered the common ink, and left the counting-house. The late afternoon sun was a blunt instrument, beating on the cobbles, raising the stink of the city—offal, smoke, humanity. It was a world of surfaces, loud, immediate, demanding.

My room, when I reached it, was a sanctuary of silence. The rolled vellum lay where I had left it, on the desk. It seemed smaller in the daylight, a quiet, dark cylinder. But its hum was still there, a sub-audible pulse in the stillness. I did not unroll it. To do so would be to enter its world again, and I needed to remain here, in this skin, for what was to come.

I changed my tunic for a cleaner, plainer one. I was not a courtier. I was a scribe summoned. The distinction mattered.

The Rue des Jardins ran along the inside of the old city wall, a street of high, narrow houses built of the pale local stone, their upper stories leaning out over the street as if whispering to each other. It was quieter here. The sounds of the lower city were a muffled rumble. The green door was not grand, but it was solid, freshly painted, its ironwork hinges fashioned into subtle, leafy patterns. There was no crest, no mark of office.

I knocked just as the first, deep note of the Compline bell began to sound from the cathedral spire, a bronze wave rolling over the rooftops.

The door was opened by a man of perhaps fifty, tall, with a composed, watchful face. He wore a dark, serviceable robe, but the linen at his throat was fine and white. His eyes were the colour of old ale, and they took me in with a single, comprehensive glance.

“Estève,” I said.

He nodded. “Sebastian. Come in.”

He stepped aside. I entered a vestibule, then a small courtyard. A cloister walk ran around three sides, enclosing a square of tended herbs—rosemary, thyme, sage—their scents sharp in the evening air. A single lantern hung from a bracket, casting a soft, contained light. It was a space of deliberate peace, of ordered retreat. This was not a merchant’s house.

Sebastian led me across the courtyard and through another door into a room that was part library, part study. Shelves held codices and scrolls, not ledgers. A large table was strewn with open books, a celestial sphere of polished brass stood in one corner, and in another, an astrolabe on a stand caught the lamplight. The air smelled of parchment, of cedar oil for preserving leather, and that same, faint orris root.

A man rose from a chair by the cold fireplace. He was older than Sebastian, perhaps sixty, with a lean, ascetic face framed by close-cropped grey hair. He wore a simple black robe, but of exquisite wool, and at his neck was a thin silver chain from which hung a small, polished lens of rock crystal. His eyes were a pale, watery blue, and they held an expression of intense, focused curiosity.

“Thank you, Sebastian,” he said. His voice was dry, precise, without warmth but without hostility. It was the voice of a man used to examination.

Sebastian withdrew, closing the door silently.

“You are the scribe,” the man said. It was not a question.

“I am.”

“I am called Arnaud.” He gestured to a chair opposite his. “Sit. You will take some wine? It is from the Agenais. Not the best, but it has a truthful character.”

I nodded. He poured from a plain earthenware jug into two glass beakers—a luxury. The wine was dark, almost purple. I took a sip. It was tannic, sharp with the taste of the schist soil, a landscape in a cup.

“Truthful,” I agreed.

A ghost of a smile touched his lips. “A man who knows the taste of a place in its wine is a man who listens with more than his ears.” He set his beaker down, steepled his fingers. “The piece you transcribed last night. The *canso* of Ventadorn. Where did you learn it?”

The question, so direct, was a blade laid on the table between us. I felt the line pull taut. “I did not learn it. I remembered it.”

He leaned forward, the pale eyes brightening. “Explain.”

“I heard it. Once. A long time ago. In the Midi. A troubadour singing in the courtyard of a *castrum*. I was a child. The words… adhered.”

“And the melody? The *contrafactum*? You have remembered the precise melodic line? The *ouvert* and *clos* of the *pedes*?”

He was using the technical language of the *art de trobar*. This was no casual appreciator. This was a scholar, perhaps a practitioner.

“I remember the shape it made in the air,” I said carefully. “The rise and fall of it. Like the line of hills at dusk.”

“Hills at dusk,” he repeated softly. He stood and went to the shelves, returning with a large, flat book. He opened it. It was a fascicle of unbound parchment, ruled for musical notation. On it were neat, black *neumes*, the square notes of the modern style, arranged on a four-line staff. But above them, in a different ink, were older, subtler signs: the delicate, climbing *neumes* of the Aquitainian tradition, the ones that gestured towards pitch without defining the prison of the line. It was a palimpsest of musical time.

“This is my life’s work,” Arnaud said, his finger tracing the older notation. “The recovery of the *son*, the true sound, from the carcass of the written note. The scribes of Saint Martial, of Limoges, they knew how to suggest it. But the knowledge faded. The music was flattened, boxed, to fit the new rigidity. The memory of the sound was lost.” He looked at me. “Until last night.”

“Last night?”

“A man I employ to listen in certain taverns, to copy down the songs of the street, the *rotrouenges*, the *chansons de toile*, brought me a report. Not of a song heard, but of a song… overheard. From an open window. A single voice, singing in langue d’oc, with a melodic contour he did not recognize, but which he described with surprising acuity. He followed the sound. He saw your window. He saw you at your lectern. He is a good listener. He came to me at dawn.”

So. My private crucible had had a witness. The song had escaped my room, flown out into the night, and been caught in the net of a professional listener. The fish was not the only thing that had felt the hook. I had attracted a fisherman of a different sort.

“You are a collector,” I said.

“I am a conservator,” he corrected. “The world is a fire, Estève. It consumes. It turns the complex into ash, the nuanced into simple heat. My work is to rescue the fragile things from the flames. The old songs. The old knowledge. The old ways of hearing and seeing. They are the true history, not the chronicles of kings and battles. Those are only the noise of the fire. The song is the shape of what is being burned.”

His words resonated in the hollow place my night’s work had carved within me. He was speaking my own, unformed thought. He saw the past not as a ledger, but as a vanishing symphony.

“Why summon me?” I asked. “You have your listener’s report.”

“Because a report is a description of a ghost. You are the medium through which the ghost spoke. You did not simply recall a lyric. You recalled a *performance*. The breath, the phrasing, the *silence* between the notes. That is what is lost. That is what I seek. You are not a singer. You are a scribe. Yet you performed an act of *memoria* so complete it bypassed the mind and spoke through the voice. How?”

I looked at the wine in my glass, the dark pool of it. “I do not know how. I only know that when I write to remember—truly remember—I must… vacate myself. The parchment becomes the place. The ink becomes the time. I am not writing words. I am opening a door.”

Arnaud was utterly still. “A door,” he breathed. “Yes. And what lies behind the door?”

“A country,” I said, the word leaving me before I could weigh it. “My country. Not a map, but the taste of the air. The weight of the light. The sound a river makes over a certain kind of stone.”

“And you can do this at will?”

“No. It is not a willed thing. It is a… surrender. The text must be a key. The right shape. The right resonance. Ventadorn’s sorrow was a key. It fit a lock within me. It turned.”

He stood and paced to the window, looking out into the dark courtyard. “Master Thomas believes you are an asset of diminishing value. He fears the crack in your neutrality. He is right to fear it, but wrong about the value.” He turned back. “I am a servant of a powerful man. A man who understands that power is not only territory and taxes. It is knowledge. The right song, sung at the right moment, can sway a council, soothe a rebellion, sanctify a treaty. The old songs, the true songs, are a technology of influence. My patron wishes to possess this technology.”

The cold clarity of it washed over me. This was not about preservation for its own sake. It was about acquisition. The fire he spoke of saving things from was also a fire he wished to control. I was no longer just a scribe who remembered. I was a potential instrument in a different, more subtle kind of warfare.

“You wish me to remember for you,” I said.

“I wish you to *transcribe* for me. But in your way. Not copying. Not reconstruction. Resurrection. There are texts. Fragments. A line of melody here, a torn stanza there, in archives, in private *librairies*, even etched on the walls of forgotten chapels. They are ciphers. I believe you can decode them. You can hear the full music in the broken phrase.”

He went to his desk, unlocked a small, iron-bound coffer. From it, he drew a single piece of parchment, small, its edges frayed. He brought it to me.

It was a fragment, perhaps six inches square. The script was a hurried, late Carolingian minuscule, faded to brown. It was not a literary text. It was a line of music, with words beneath it in a langue d’oc so archaic the words were almost shapes rather than language.

*…alba… es l’alba…*

…dawn… it is the dawn…

An *alba*. A dawn song. The song of lovers parting at daybreak. The rest was torn away, eaten by time or damp.

“This was found walled up in a monastery near Toulouse,” Arnaud said, his voice hushed. “It is perhaps two hundred years old. No one knows its tune. No one can sing it. It is a skeleton. Can you… open the door for this?”

I took the fragment. The parchment was brittle, fragile. The ink was a ghost of itself. I looked at the *neumes*, little more than angled ticks and dots. They were silent. Dead.

I closed my eyes. Not to remember, but to listen. To the hum of the room. To my own breath. I tried to empty myself, as I had the night before. To make a space for the echo.

Nothing came.

Only the pressure of Arnaud’s gaze, the weight of his expectation. The scent of orris root, now cloying. The awareness of the transaction being proposed. I was to be his divining rod, his dowser for lost songs. My peculiar wound, my homesickness made flesh in memory, was to be mined for its utility.

I opened my eyes. “No. Not here. Not now. It is not a trick to be performed.”

He did not look disappointed. He looked intrigued. “Of course. The conditions must be right. The key must fit. You will take it. Study it. When the door opens, you will transcribe what you hear. Fully. In both the old *neumes* and the new notation. A complete record.”

“And in return?”

“Master Thomas will find your work increasingly profitable. Your debts, if you have any, will be quietly resolved. You will have access to materials—vellum, inks, the finest quills—beyond a common scribe’s means. And you will be preserving a world that would otherwise vanish. You will be giving voice to the silent.”

It was a good offer. It appealed to the scribe, to the exile, to the man who had just spent a night in fervent communion with a ghost. It was also a trap. Once I began, I would be bound to him, to his patron. My inner landscape would become a surveyed territory, its resources mapped and extracted. The song would become a commodity. The fish, once reeled in, would be weighed, measured, and served on a platter of power.

I looked at the fragment in my hand. *…es l’alba…* It is the dawn. The lovers parting. The light breaking, ending the secret night.

My night of translation was over. A new day was indeed breaking, and it was revealing the contours of a different kind of struggle. Not against forgetting, but against appropriation.

“I will consider it,” I said, placing the fragment carefully on the table between us.

Arnaud’s pale eyes assessed me. He saw the resistance. He nodded slowly. “Consideration is wise. But do not consider too long. The fire burns, Estève. What is not saved is consumed. And remember: you have already cast a light. Lights attract more than just conservators. They attract those who fear the dark you illuminate. My offer is protection, as well as patronage. The world of Master Thomas is not safe for a man whose parchment hums.”

He rose, a signal the audience was over. Sebastian must have been waiting just outside the door, for he entered instantly to see me out.

In the courtyard, the night was full. The herbs released their scent under the cloak of dew. The Compline bell had long since faded. Sebastian opened the green door.

“A good night to you,” he said, his tone neutral.

I stepped out into the Rue des Jardins. The door closed behind me with a soft, final click. I stood in the darkness, the taste of the Agenais wine still on my tongue, the words *es l’alba* echoing in the vault of my mind.

I had gone to the green door thinking I might find an ally, a fellow rememberer. I had found a curator of echoes, who served a master of whispers. My song had been heard, and it had been given a price.

As I walked back through the sleeping city, the weight of the two worlds pressed on me. The world of the counting-house, of cages and ledgers. And this new, shadowed world of green doors and stolen songs, where memory was a currency and the past a weapon to be forged in the present.

The fish was diving for the deep, dark places, just as I had foreseen. But I had been wrong about the nature of the depths. The deepest water was not the past, nor the indifferent city. It was the present moment, here and now, where the hooks of others waited, baited with the very things I thought I owned: my memory, my loss, my song.

The line was still cutting through the water. But now I could feel other lines, other tensions, crossing mine in the dark. The struggle was no longer solitary. The sea was full of fishermen.



---

# Chapter 17

## **Chapter 17**

The counting-house was a cage of bone-dry air, and I was a creature of damp vellum and river-ink. I sat at my high desk, the ledger open before me. The columns of figures were a silent, numeric chant, a *canso* of profit and loss in a tongue I could parse but never feel. *Livres, sous, deniers.* The ink here was not oak gall and iron, but a sooty black, ground from lamp-ash and gum. It smelled of nothing but dust. My smoothing stone was a lump of pumice, cold and sterile. I ran my thumb over its porous surface, and thought of beeswax, of the faint greasy warmth it left on the skin.

Master Thomas was a silhouette against the bright window, reviewing a bond. The scratch of his quill was the only sound, a dry insect-rustle in the stillness. It was the third day since the green door had closed behind me. Three days of this deliberate, punishing normalcy. I had returned to my cell above the stables, to my bread and cheese, to the ledger’s tyranny. I had spoken to no one of the Conservatori, of the man with the ageless eyes and the offer that was not an offer. To speak of it would be to make it real in this world, to let that shadowed world bleed into the clean, ruled lines of Master Thomas’s domain. It would be a contamination.

And yet, the contamination was already within me. It hummed.

*Es l’alba.* The dawn. The parting. The lady’s cold face turning away. The melody of it was a splinter in my mind, working its way deeper with every silent hour. I had not sung it, not once, since leaving the Rue des Jardins. I had locked it away, as one locks away a vial of poison or a holy relic. But a locked thing has a presence. It exerts a pressure. I could feel it now, a tightness behind my eyes, a resonance in the hollow of my chest, as if my ribs were the sounding-box of a neglected instrument.

“Estève.”

I started. Master Thomas had not turned. His voice was flat, addressed to the bond in his hands.

“Master.”

“The entry for the Genoese silk shipment. The duty is calculated at the old rate. The king’s tariff increased at the new moon.” He finally turned. His face was its usual mask of contained efficiency, but his eyes held a particular, needle-like focus. “An error of inattention. See to it.”

“Yes, Master.”

I found the entry. The error was mine. A small thing, a matter of a few sous. In the ledger of the counting-house, it was a pebble. In the ledger of my worth here, it was a crack in the foundation. He had not missed it. He missed nothing. The correction took a moment. I sanded the wrong figure, blew the grit away, inscribed the new one. The action was rote, but my hand felt clumsy, a stranger’s hand. It wanted to be shaping the graceful, looping ascenders of Occitan, not these stark, utilitarian numerals.

The silence returned, thicker now. It was a silence of observation. I could feel his gaze on the back of my neck, a cold spot. He was weighing me. The fish, returned from its nocturnal wandering, now lay gasping on the bank. He was probing its gills, testing its vitality.

“You are quiet these last days,” he said, the insect-scratch of his quill resuming. “The night air in the city does not agree with you?”

The hook was baited with casual concern. I kept my eyes on the ledger. “The air is the same, Master. My thoughts have been… occupied with the complexities of the Flemish wool accounts.”

A lie. The wool accounts were simple arithmetic. The complexity was in the echo of a 12th-century melody, in the memory of a voice saying *you have already cast a light.*

“Occupation is the enemy of error,” he said, a proverb delivered like a coin of known weight. “See that your occupation remains here, within these walls. The city at night is a ledger of a different sort. Its columns are shadows. Its sums are often paid in blood.”

A warning. Plain, but layered. *I know you go out. I do not know where, or why. But I know the night is not for you.* Was it the general warning of a master for a servant? Or had Sebastian’s neutral eyes reported my visit? The green door had felt secret, but in a city of whispers, no door was truly closed.

“I understand, Master.”

He gave a short, satisfied nod, and the subject was closed. The threat, however, hung in the bone-dry air, another tension in the web. I was a line being pulled from two directions. Master Thomas, with his cage of law and ledger. The Conservatori, with its gilded hook of patronage and peril. And between them, the song, my own private fault line.

The day bled on, hour by measured hour. The light from the window crawled across the oak floorboards. A client came, was received with frigid courtesy, departed. A messenger brought a letter sealed with a blob of red wax. The ordinary machinery of the world. But my inner ear was tuned to a different frequency. Every footfall in the street outside, every call of a hawker, seemed like a potential signal. I waited for a touch on my shoulder, for a note slipped into my hand, for the quiet man Sebastian to materialize in the courtyard. The offer was a door left ajar. I expected it to be pushed fully open.

It did not happen. The Conservatori, it seemed, understood the value of patience. They had cast their line. Now they would let the fish tire itself against the current.

By the time the Angelus bell rang, my nerves were a tangled knot. The silence from that quarter was more unnerving than a summons would have been. It left the field of my imagination open, and I populated it with every possible consequence of refusal. The fire burns. What is not saved is consumed. Were they saving, or were they the fire? The distinction, in the dark, seemed perilously thin.

Released from my cage, I walked. Not toward the river, not toward the green door. I walked aimlessly, a leaf caught in the eddies of the city’s twilight. I found myself in the square before the cathedral of Saint-Étienne. The great west front was a cliff of stone in the dying light, the rose window a dark, unblinking eye. The faithful trickled in for Vespers. The sound of the organ groaned through the open doors, a vast, wooden breath.

I stood by the empty market cross, watching. This was the other memory, the sanctioned memory. The memory carved in stone, sung in Latin, burned in incense. It was a memory without faces, without the taste of Agenais wine or the scent of night-herbs. It was a memory of doctrine, not of *dolor*. A memory for the multitude, not for the solitary heart.

A figure detached itself from the shadow of a buttress and walked toward me. My breath caught. But it was not Sebastian. It was a friar, a Franciscan in his grey habit, his face young and earnest beneath his tonsure.

“Peace be with you, brother,” he said, his eyes kind. “Do you seek solace?”

I looked at him, this purveyor of universal comfort. What solace could he offer for a sorrow that was seven centuries old, for a love that had never been mine? He could absolve me of sin, but not of memory. His was a trade in forgiveness. Mine, it seemed, was becoming a trade in preservation.

“I seek only the quiet,” I said, my voice rough.

He smiled, a gentle, practiced smile. “God’s house is open to you. His quiet is the deepest of all.”

He moved on, a grey ghost melting back into the stream of worshippers. I did not follow. God’s quiet was not my quiet. My quiet was the hum of a parchment waiting for a song it had never been meant to hold. It was the silence after the last note of *es l’alba* has faded, a silence so complete it becomes a presence.

I turned from the cathedral and its promises of mass salvation. My path took me, as if by a homing instinct, to the Street of the Scribes. It was nearly dark. The shutters were closed, the stalls packed away. But here, the smell lingered: the animal tang of glue, the acid bite of urine used for bleaching, the sweet, rotten-fruit smell of aging paper. Here, the tools of my other life were mere commerce. Here, a perfect vellum sheet was worth so many sous per square foot, its value in its flawlessness, not its potential.

I paused before the shop of Maître Albert, the old illuminator. A faint, warm light leaked from his shutters. He often worked late, his eyes too worn for daylight detail. On an impulse, I knocked softly.

A long pause, then the sound of a bolt being drawn. The door opened a crack, and Maître Albert’s seamed face, smudged with azurite blue, peered out. His eyes, milky with cataracts, widened in recognition.

“The rememberer,” he grunted. “You come at the hour of ghosts. Do you bring one?”

“I come as a man adrift, Maître. May I enter?”

He stepped back, opening the door fully. His single room was a cave of wonders and chaos. Parchments were pinned to every vertical surface. Jars of pigment lined shelves: malachite green, vermilion, gold so fine it was like captured sunlight. Half-finished initials sprawled across a tilted desk—a ‘D’ inhabited by a sly-faced dragon, an ‘O’ that was a window onto a miniature sea. The air was thick with the smell of egg tempera and charcoal.

“Adrift,” he echoed, shuffling to a small hearth where a pot of glue simmered. “In my experience, men are only adrift when they have lost sight of both shore and star. Which have you lost, Estève?”

I had never told him my name. The fact that he knew it did not surprise me. In this street, little went unmarked. “I am unsure of the nature of the shore. And the stars… they seem to give conflicting directions.”

He ladled a little glue into a ceramic cup, added hot water from a kettle, and pushed it toward me. “Drink. It will steady your hand. Not for your hand? For your spirit, then. Now. Conflicting stars. You have had an offer.”

I sipped the weak, glutinous brew. It was tasteless, warm. “How did you know?”

He cackled, a dry sound like pages turning. “A man like you has only one thing of value. It is not your skill with a ledger. When such a man grows quiet, looks haunted, it is because someone has placed a price on that one thing. Was it the Bishop’s man? No, too crude. The University? They would have flattered you with titles. It was a private collector. One with… esoteric tastes.”

He saw too much. “A society. They call themselves the Conservatori.”

Maître Albert went very still. He set down his own cup carefully. All the amusement bled from his face, leaving something old and cautious. “Ah,” he said, the single syllable heavy. “The Curators of Echoes. So they have heard your parchment hum.”

“You know them.”

“I know *of* them. They have approached me, once, long ago. For a… specific commission. A page from a very old, very forbidden text. They wished it not copied, but *re-created*. The vellum, the ink, the hand—all must be of the period. A perfect, undetectable ghost.” He looked at his own hands, stained with the colors of creation and deceit. “I refused.”

“Why?”

“Because they do not want the thing itself. They want the *effect* of the thing. There is a difference. A great difference. To forge a document for a fool who wishes to boast of his antiquity is one thing. It is commerce. To forge it for those who wish to plant it in the soil of the present, to let its roots twist through the foundations of what is known… that is gardening. And I am no gardener. I am a craftsman.” He fixed his milky gaze on me. “What did they offer you?”

“Patronage. Protection. A place to remember.”

“A cage,” he corrected softly. “Gilded, perhaps. But a cage nonetheless. They will feed you your own memories until you forget how to find new ones. You will become a living reliquary for the sorrows of dead men. Is that what you want?”

“I want the songs not to be lost.” The words felt weak, childish, in the face of his stark clarity.

“And so you would hand them to men who see them as tools? A song is a living breath. A tool is a dead weight. They will take your breath and forge it into a key, or a weapon, or a lock. They will use your *dolor* to open doors you never meant to approach.” He leaned forward, his voice dropping to a whisper. “Listen to me, rememberer. Memory is identity. You are what you hold. If you sell what you hold, you sell the ground beneath your own feet. You will become an echo of an echo. A ghost in their machine.”

His words struck the very center of my fear. They gave form to the formless dread that had coiled in me since leaving the Rue des Jardins. The Conservatori did not want Estève the clerk, the rememberer. They wanted the vessel. They wanted the song, and the vessel could be discarded once it was emptied, or refilled with another vintage.

“What choice do I have?” The question was a plea. “Master Thomas’s world is a desert. It will kill this thing in me by inches, by silence. The Conservatori, at least, would give it room to breathe.”

“Would they?” Maître Albert sighed, a long, weary exhalation. “There is a third way. But it is the hardest way. It is the way of the wanderer. The way of the troubadour himself, who owned nothing but his cloak and his voice. You must keep your song, and you must keep yourself. You must make your memory a moving target. You must sing it to the air, not inscribe it for a vault. You must give it away, to those who will hear it and forget it, or remember it wrong. You must let it live in the imperfect, mortal world, where it can change, and grow, and die a natural death. That is how a song truly survives. Not in a collector’s cabinet, but in the wild.”

It was a terrifying prospect. To release the only thing that was truly mine into the careless world. To trust it to the wind.

“I am not a troubadour,” I said. “I have no court, no lady, no courage.”

“You have a song,” he said simply. “That is all a troubadour ever had. The rest is costume.”

The glue in my cup had formed a skin. I looked at it, a perfect, translucent membrane. So fragile. So easily ruptured.

“They will not simply let me walk away,” I said. “They have seen the light. They will seek to control it, or snuff it out.”

Maître Albert nodded slowly. “Then you must become a shadow. You must learn to hold your song so quietly that even you can barely hear it. You must make your life in the ledger-world so impeccable, so dull, that you become invisible to them. You must let them believe the light has gone out. It is a harder discipline than any they would impose. It is the discipline of the secret heart.”

We sat in silence for a long time. The glue-pot bubbled on the hearth. The dragon in the ‘D’ seemed to watch us, its jeweled eye catching the flicker of the candle.

Finally, I rose. My limbs were heavy, but the knot in my chest had loosened, replaced by a cold, clear resolution. “Thank you, Maître.”

“Do not thank me. I have given you a burden, not a gift.” He looked up, his old eyes sharp. “One more thing. If they sense your retreat, they may press you. They may use a lever. Everyone has a lever. Find yours, and guard it.”

I left him to his colors and his ghosts. The night was fully come, the sky a velvet black. The third way. The way of the secret heart. It felt impossible. It felt like the only path that did not end in my own erasure.

As I walked back to the counting-house, to my cell above the stables, I practiced. I held the line *De moi doleros vos chant* in my mind, but I did not sing it. I did not trace its melody. I let it sit, inert, a lump of unshaped meaning. I made my face a blank page. I rehearsed the motions of the coming day: the ledger, the pumice stone, the dry ink. I would be Estève the clerk, diligent, unremarkable, a man of numbers and silence.

But deep within, beneath the performance, the song hummed. Not loudly. Not a call. A murmur. A steady, subterranean pulse. My secret heart. My moving target.

I reached the stable door. The scent of hay and horse was a blunt, honest thing. I climbed the narrow stairs. My cell was as I had left it: the narrow cot, the small table, the stub of a candle. A place for a man who owned nothing.

I sat on the edge of the cot. The fatigue of the day, of the mental siege, washed over me. I closed my eyes. In the darkness behind my lids, I did not see the ledger. I saw the parchment on the oak lectern, pale and waiting. I saw the river-delta of my failures. I saw the smooth, hungry surface, ready to drink the truth of a feeling.

I had come to the city to save a memory from oblivion. I had found that oblivion was not the only enemy. There were those who would save it too dearly, who would embalm it in patronage and purpose until its life was gone. And there were those, like Master Thomas, in whose world such memories were simply irrelevant, a kind of noise to be filtered out.

My task was no longer to save the song from being forgotten.
My task was to save it from being *used*.

I lay back. The straw mattress crackled. From the street below, faint and far away, came the sound of a night-watchman’s call. *All is well.*

It was a lie. Nothing was well. The lines were drawn. The fishermen were patient. And I, the fish, had decided not to bite, not to flee, but to learn how to breathe a different kind of water. To hold the light inside, where no net could scoop it up, no hook could find purchase.

But Maître Albert’s warning echoed. *Everyone has a lever.*

As I drifted into a thin, uneasy sleep, the face that came to me was not Bernart’s lady, nor the ageless man in the green room. It was the face of the boy, Luc, in the scriptorium of Saint-Michel-de-Cuxa. His look of shared guilt as we hid the ruined page. His simple, devastating question: *What do you remember?*

He was my only confidant. The only living soul who knew the nature of my remembering. A boy in a distant monastery, a secret shared in the shadow of a pine forest.

A cold certainty seeped into my dreams.

If the Conservatori sought a lever, they would not look in ledgers or in taverns. They would look for a weakness written in a older, more vulnerable hand. They would look for the first and only friend of the rememberer.

And I had no way to warn him.



---

# Chapter 18

## **Chapter 18**

The city did not sleep. It merely changed its breath. The sharp, day-time exhalations of commerce and argument softened into the low, congested inhalations of shadow-work and watchfulness. I heard it through the warped floorboards of my room, a susurrus of wet wheels on cobbles, of a drunkard’s mumbled oath, of a shutter being fastened not against the night air, but against the night itself. I lay on the crackling straw, the taste of Maître Albert’s wine still a faint, sour ghost on my tongue. His words were not ghosts. They were stones, settling in the pit of my stomach.

*Everyone has a lever.*

The face of Luc, pale and earnest in the mountain dusk, was painted on the darkness behind my eyes. It was not a memory I had chosen to summon. It had risen, unbidden, like a corpse in a still pond. I had thought him safe, insulated by distance and stone. A secret shared in the sacred silence of a scriptorium was a secret buried in consecrated ground. I saw now the arrogance of that. There was no consecration deep enough. The Conservatori did not dig with shovels; they dug with whispers, with ledgers, with the quiet, administrative pressure that could make a mountain monastery feel the weight of a distant city’s curiosity.

My resolve, so solid and defiant as I left the tavern, began to crumble at its edges. To hold the light inside was a fine, poetic defiance. But it assumed the darkness outside remained impersonal, a mere absence. It was not. It was a patient, sentient thing, and it would not starve. It would go where the light had been, trace its old paths, probe the places it had warmed.

Luc was such a place.

The thought was a physical chill. I sat up, the rough wool of my blanket falling away. The room was a box of gloom. My few possessions—the satchel, the worn cloak, the small knife for sharpening quills—were mere lumps of deeper shadow. They offered no counsel. The city’s night-murmur pressed in, a language I was only beginning to decipher, and in it I heard only one phrase, repeated in a hundred different tones: *a lever, a lever, a lever.*

I had to write to him. A warning, couched in the only code we shared: the language of the scriptorium. I could write of a troublesome new parchment, of an over-curious corrector, of the importance of diligent solitude. He would understand. He had the same mind, the same fear of violation. I fumbled for my tinderbox, struck a spark to a tallow dip. The flame guttered, then steadied, painting the walls with frantic, leaping shapes. It was a pathetic light, a peasant’s light, but it was mine. I pulled a scrap of paper from my satchel—not good vellum, but coarse stuff, fit for accounts or lists. My ink was a small, hard cake in a seashell. I spat into the shell, grinding the cake with my finger until it yielded a thin, grey-black fluid.

The quill in my hand felt alien. It was just a goose feather, stripped and cut, but its weight was the weight of a weapon I did not know how to wield. I hovered over the paper. The first mark would be a breach. It would create a thread, physical and traceable, between this room and the Pyrenees. Was that not exactly what I feared? Yet to do nothing was to leave him naked.

I began. *Frater Luc, salve.* The letters were tight, anxious. *I write from a city of stone that remembers nothing. The work here is… scrutinized. There are new masters, who see not the beauty of a line, but the use to which it may be put. They are archivists of the living breath, and they would pin it like a butterfly.* I paused, the quill dripping a small, spreading star upon the page. This was too direct. It was a scream in a silent library. I crumpled the paper, fed it to the tallow flame. It curled, blackened, became a fragile, glowing ghost before dissolving into ash.

I took another scrap. Simpler. *The mountain air must be clear. Hold to your silence. It is a deeper well than any city cistern. Do not speak of our studies. A curious party may inquire. You know nothing.* I signed it with a single letter: the Occitan *‘I’* for *‘Ivo.’* It was a frail shield. If the Conservatori came to Saint-Michel, a boy’s denial would be as insubstantial as mist. They would have a letter from the Bishop, a suggestion from a patron. They would ask their gentle, inexorable questions. And Luc, with his honest eyes and his terror of damnation for a lie, would break. He would tell them of the ruined page, of the remembered song, of the strange oblate who could hear the dead sing.

The certainty of it was a vise around my lungs. I blew out the candle. The darkness rushed back, complete and suffocating. My plan—to be a stone in the river, to let their nets and hooks pass over me—was the fantasy of a child. I had connections. Fragile, human connections. And they were vulnerabilities. My defiance had made me a fortress, but I had left the postern gate unguarded, and the name of that gate was Luc.

Sleep, when it came, was not a respite. It was a descent into a scriptorium of the mind. I stood again at the oak lectern in Cuxa, but the parchment was immense, a landscape of pale skin stretched to the horizon. Upon it, instead of Bernart’s *canso*, was a single, repeating phrase in a hand that was both mine and not mine: *What do you remember? What do you remember?* And from the shadows of the cloister, figures emerged. Not monks, but men in the dark, fine wool of the Conservatori. They did not look at me. They walked to the edges of the great parchment, took its corners in their hands, and began to fold it. They folded the vast memory into a smaller and smaller square, a neat, dense packet, until it was the size of a letter. One of them sealed it with a blank disc of wax. “For the archives,” he said, his voice the rustle of dry leaves. “Where it will be safe. Where it will be *still*.”

I woke with a gasp, the dawn a thin, grey gruel at the window. My body ached as if from a physical struggle. The dream’s logic was impeccable. This was their true purpose: not to destroy memory, but to archive it. To fold its chaotic, living expanse into a classified, silent thing. A remembered song in a living throat was a dangerous, unpredictable flame. The same song, catalogued and shelved, was a specimen. A curio. Tamed.

I rose, splashed my face with water from the jug. The face in the chipped basin was hollow-eyed, the face of a man who has seen the blueprint of his own defeat. I had been fighting the wrong enemy. Oblivion was a blunt weapon. This was something subtler, more final: a conversion of life into evidence.

I needed to move. To act. The passivity of the watched fish was a luxury I could no longer afford. I had to know if the threat to Luc was real, or merely the phantom of my own fear. And there was only one place in the city where such knowledge might flow, however contaminated its source.

***

The House of the Green Book was not a place one visited in the clear light of morning. It belonged to the liminal hours, to the time between the closing of the markets and the closing of the taverns. To go now felt like a violation of its nature, like lifting a rock to see the pale things beneath before the sun had warmed them. The street was quiet, respectable. A woman scrubbed a step. A cat dozed on a wall. The door, with its green paint peeling like old lichen, was firmly shut.

I knocked, the sound absurdly loud in the sleepy lane.
After a long moment, a slot at eye-level scraped open. A single, bloodshot eye peered out. It was not the ageless man. It was a younger, coarser face, pocked and suspicious.
“We’re closed.”
“I need to speak to… the proprietor.”
“He’s not receiving.”
“Tell him it’s the rememberer. The one from the Midi.”
The eye blinked, vanished. There was a low muttering. Minutes passed. I stood on the step, feeling exposed, a strange mushroom sprouting in the wrong soil. Finally, bolts were drawn—two, three, four. The door opened just wide enough to admit me, and I was pulled inside by a strong, ink-stained hand.

The main room was a corpse in daylight. Without the glow of lamps and the press of bodies, it was revealed as a shabby, low-ceilinged space, smelling of stale wine, old smoke, and the faint, sweet-rotten odor of decaying paper. Dust motes churned in the shafts of light spearing through shutters that were still half-closed. The ageless man sat at a table by the cold hearth, not reading, not writing. Simply sitting. He wore a simple dark robe, and without the theatricality of lamplight, he looked merely tired, and old.

“You have the timing of a tax collector,” he said, his voice dry. “This is the house’s time for forgetting. For sleep.”
“I need information.”
“A currency we trade in. But the rates are higher at this hour. The risk of clarity is greater.”
I stepped closer. “The Conservatori. Would they… investigate a person? Someone connected to me, but far from here. In a monastery.”
He steepled his fingers. The nails were clean, perfectly shaped. “The Conservatori does not ‘investigate.’ It assimilates. It understands. If a person is a thread in a tapestry they wish to comprehend, they will follow the thread. To its very beginning.”
“A boy. A novice. He knows nothing of value.”
“You are wrong,” he said softly. “He knows *you*. That is value. To them, you are a new and irregular stitch in the fabric of the known. They will trace the thread back to its origin, to see what other irregularities it is connected to. To see if the flaw is in the thread, or in the loom.” He studied me. “This is the lever you feared.”
It was not a question. I did not answer. My silence was confirmation enough.
“You wish to warn him,” the man continued. “A letter is a ribbon tying you both to a post. A messenger is a louder announcement. Your connection is your vulnerability. To act upon it is to make it real, to give it a shape they can grasp.”
“So I do nothing?”
“I did not say that.” He leaned back, the chair creaking. “You misunderstand their method. They do not ride in with soldiers. They send a scholar. A friendly cleric with letters of introduction. He will share a meal, admire the architecture, express a pious interest in the scriptorium’s work. He will ask gentle questions about unusual talents, about the beauty of preserving the old songs. He will be charming. And your young friend, in his awe and his fear of giving offense to such an eminent guest, will tell him everything. Not out of malice. Out of a desire to be *seen* as worthy. The lever is not pain. It is recognition.”
The scene painted itself in my mind with dreadful clarity. I saw it: the visiting scholar, kind-eyed, praising Luc’s hand; Luc, flushing with pride, the secret swelling in his chest until it became a confession, a shared wonder.
“How do I stop it?”
“You cannot stop the inquiry. It is already in motion, or soon will be. Maître Albert’s report will have… ripples.” He paused, choosing his words as one chooses stones for a path. “But you can change the story they find.”
“How?”
“By providing a better one.” His gaze was unwavering. “You are the anomaly. The source. If the anomaly presents itself for study—voluntarily, openly—the need to pick at the distant threads lessens. They have the root. Why bother with the leaf?”
A cold understanding dawned. “You want me to go to them.”
“I want nothing. I am describing a strategy. You have decided not to be their scribe. A noble refusal. But it has made you a mystery. Mysteries are hunted. If you become a known quantity—a difficult, perhaps disappointing, but ultimately *known* quantity—you become less interesting. You give them a narrative. ‘The gifted but unstable oblate from Cuxa, prone to fanciful notions of memory, now safely employed on mundane tasks.’ They can file you. Your friend becomes a footnote, a youthful impression of a peculiar elder brother. Harmless.”
It was a repulsive, brilliant logic. It meant surrender. Not the defiant, internal surrender I had envisioned, but an active, humiliating capitulation. To walk into the scriptorium of the Conservatori, to offer myself up for their inspection, to let them classify me and thereby drain me of my danger.
“And if their study… damages the memory?”
He spread his hands. “Is the song in your head, or on the page? You told me it was in the breath, in the feeling. Can they archive a feeling? They can archive your transcription of it. They can label you. They cannot touch the thing itself, unless you give it to them. This is the deeper game. You let them win the board, while you keep the king hidden.”
It was what Maître Albert had implied. A performance. But the cost of the performance was to make my life a lie, to reduce my gift to a symptom, to become a harmless fool in their records.
“I need time to think.”
“Time,” he said, a hint of his old, ironic smile returning, “is the one currency we are all running out of. They will not wait for your epiphany. The machinery is turning. Every day you delay is a day their scholar draws closer to your mountain.”
He stood, a signal of dismissal. “The choice is between two kinds of forgetting. The kind they will impose upon you—a systematic, archival forgetting—or the kind you choose for yourself: to be forgotten *by* them. To become a dull page in their ledger, so that the living text remains yours alone.”
I left through the heavy door, which shut behind me with a sound like a tomb sealing. The morning sun was stronger now, but it held no warmth. I walked without direction, the man’s words turning in my mind, a mill grinding my resolve to powder.

For three days, I was a ghost in the city. I performed my duties for Master Thomas with a mechanical precision that drew no comment. I ate. I slept fitfully. I watched the street from my window, seeing agents of the Conservatori in every loiterer, every passing clerk. The pressure was an atmospheric weight, a storm that would not break. I was waiting for the touch of the lever.

It came on the fourth day, not with a scholar at my door, but with a letter.
It was delivered not to my lodgings, but to the counting-house. Master Thomas brought it to me himself, his expression unreadable. The parchment was fine, creamy, and sealed not with a blank disc, but with a clear, sharp impression: a key lying atop a closed book.
“From the Collegium Conservatorum,” Master Thomas said, his voice neutral. “Addressed to you. It seems your patrons are impatient for your decision.”
My hands were steady as I took it. I broke the seal. The script within was a masterpiece of controlled elegance, each letter a monument to discipline.

*To Ivo, formerly of Saint-Michel-de-Cuxa,*
*Greetings in the name of shared preservation.*
*Your period of reflection is respected. However, the work of Memory does not pause. A project of singular importance has arisen, one which aligns precisely with the unique aptitudes you are reported to possess. It concerns the recovery of a fragmentary *planh* from the Languedoc, believed lost after the death of its composer. The fragment is held in a private collection here in the city. We require a sensitive ear to hear what the vellum no longer clearly says.*
*This is not an offer of employment. It is an invitation to collaboration. A test, if you will, of a principle: that the past may be served without being enslaved.*
*You will be compensated as a consulting scholar. Your independence will remain your own.*
*We await your reply at the Collegium by the end of the week.*
*In Service to the Unbroken Line,*
*The Prefect of Acquisitions.*

It was a masterstroke. It ignored my refusal. It bypassed the threat. It offered not a demand, but a tantalizing puzzle. A *planh*—a lament—from my own region, a fragment believed lost. It was the one bait I could not refuse. They had not found my lever; they had *built* one, crafted from the very substance of my devotion. They had studied me, and they had written a letter that was a perfect mirror of my own desires, reflecting them back with the cold polish of strategy.

This was the false victory.
I had thought I understood the game. I had thought my defiance—my internal, silent defiance—was a form of strength. I had built a citadel within myself, and I believed it impregnable. But they did not lay siege to citadels. They built beautiful, irresistible bridges to them. They offered not conquest, but collaboration. Not chains, but a shared, sacred purpose.

And in doing so, they would make me complicit in my own archiving. To work on the *planh* would be to validate their methods, to lend my gift to their project. I would be saving a memory, yes, but I would be doing it *for them*, within their walls, under their name. The memory would be theirs. I would be the instrument of its transfer from the wilds of my mind to the ordered shelves of their collection.

The collapse was not of walls, but of meaning. My entire stance—the lonely rememberer guarding the flame—was rendered childish. They offered a better way, a cleaner, more efficient salvation. Who was I to deny a lost *planh* its home, simply to protect my own sense of purity?

I held the letter, this elegant, poison-tipped arrow, and felt the foundations of the last seventeen chapters of my life crumble into dust. I had built an identity on resistance. They had made resistance irrelevant by offering everything I thought I wanted, on terms that subtly, irrevocably, made me theirs.

Master Thomas was still watching me. “Well?” he said. “Does it please you?”
The word *‘please’* was a tiny, exquisite torture. “It is a great honor,” I heard myself say, my voice distant.
“I thought it might be.” He nodded, a man of business who understood incentives. “A man’s talents should find their proper market.”

I walked out of the counting-house at the day’s end, the letter folded inside my tunic. It lay against my chest like a second, colder heart. The city’s sounds were a blur. I found myself at the river, watching the water carry the day’s refuse toward the sea. The fishermen were there, mending their nets with timeless, patient motions. I was no longer the fish. I was the fish that had seen the lure, understood its artifice, and was now measuring the sweetness of the bait against the steel of the hook.

The false victory was this: I had believed I could choose between defiance and surrender. The deeper truth, revealed through the lens of memory and identity, was that the choice itself was the trap. To defiantly remember for myself was to make my memory a solitary, fading echo. To surrender and remember for them was to turn memory into a commodity. Both paths led to a kind of death. The self I had built—the rememberer as rebel—could not survive contact with their world. It had to shatter.

And in the shattering, a terrible, clear space opened.
I had been asking the wrong question. The question was not *how do I remember?* Nor was it *for whom do I remember?*
The question was: *What is remembered for?*

Bernart did not sing his sorrow to have it archived. He sang to have it *heard*. To connect. The memory was not an end in itself; it was a bridge between two souls, across time. My gift was not a storage vault. It was a conduit.

A plan began to form in the ruins of my old resolve. It was not a plan of resistance or of surrender. It was a plan of transference. Of theft, and of gift.

I would accept their invitation. I would go to the Collegium Conservatorum. I would work on their *planh*. I would be the model collaborator. And I would remember it. Not just the fragment they held, but the whole song, as it lived in the air of its own time. I would remember it perfectly, completely.

And then I would give it away.
Not to them. To the city. To the air.

I would find a singer. A street singer with a true voice and a hungry heart. I would teach him the *planh*, the whole of it, not as a text, but as a breath, a feeling. I would pour the memory into a living vessel outside their walls, beyond their control. I would turn their recovered fragment into a living, spreading song. I would make their archival triumph into a seed, and I would plant it in the common, untamed soil where it could grow wild again.

It was a risk that dwarfed all others. It would mean betraying their trust utterly. If discovered, it would mean ruin, perhaps worse. But it would not be defiance. It would be a deeper form of fidelity. It would be remembering *for* the memory itself, setting it free to find its own purpose.

I looked at the river, at the endless, carrying water. The net was not the only tool. There was also the current.

I had my answer. I would go to them. I would walk into the heart of their machine. And I would use their own project to break their most fundamental rule: that memory must be kept, controlled, and owned.

I turned from the water, the letter crisp in my hand. The hook was in my mouth. Now I would pull, not away, but forward, with all my strength, to see what—or who—I could drag into the light.

The lever was mine to pull, too. And the pivot point was a song they believed they had already caught.



---

# Chapter 19

## **Chapter 19**

The Collegium Conservatorum was a fortress of silence.

It stood on the Isle of the Scholars, a great, grey block of dressed stone that seemed to absorb sound rather than echo it. Its windows were narrow, vertical slits, like the embrasures in a keep. The only ornament was a single, worn bas-relief above the massive oak door: a figure with a stylus in one hand and a scroll in the other, its features smoothed to anonymity by centuries of weather. It did not welcome. It received.

I stood before it, the letter from Magister Alaric a cold weight against my chest. The hook was in my mouth. Now, the pulling.

A porter in a dun-coloured robe admitted me without a word, his eyes passing over me as if I were a piece of furniture being delivered. The entrance hall was a shock of calculated emptiness. The floor was pale, veined marble, so highly polished it reflected the vaulted ceiling in a ghostly, inverted twin. The air was cool, dry, and scentless—stripped of dust, of mildew, of life. It was the air of a tomb that had never known a body, only the idea of one.

I was led to a small antechamber. “Wait,” the porter said, and the word seemed to fall onto the stone floor and shatter. I waited. The silence here was not an absence; it was a substance. It pressed against the ears, a low, constant pressure. I found myself listening for the scuff of my own heel, the rustle of my own sleeve, just to prove I had not gone deaf.

A door opened, soundless on oiled hinges. Magister Alaric entered.

He was as I remembered from the riverbank: a man of precise angles, his grey hair cut close to his skull, his robe of fine, black wool falling in straight lines. But here, in his own domain, he seemed larger, his presence filling the sterile room.

“Scribe Arren,” he said. His voice was moderate, pleasant, yet it carried in the quiet like a chisel tap on stone. “You have come. We are pleased.”

“I have come to serve the memory,” I said, the rehearsed line feeling like a pebble in my mouth.

A faint, approving line appeared at the corner of his mouth. “Indeed. That is the only reason any of us are here. Come.”

He led me through a labyrinth of corridors. We passed closed doors of dark wood, each bearing a small bronze plaque with a number. No names. Through one half-open door, I glimpsed a scriptorium: rows of high desks, each with a shaded lamp, a scribe bent in absolute silence, their pens moving with a terrible, insect-like diligence. There was no shared breath here, no collective hum of concentration as in the guild’s workshop. Each scribe was an island in a sea of quiet.

“Discipline is the vessel,” Alaric said softly, noting my gaze. “Emotion, inspiration, even interpretation—these are volatile. They evaporate. Discipline is the alembic that condenses truth into a permanent form.”

We descended a spiral staircase, the air growing cooler still. At the bottom was another door, this one of iron-bound oak. Alaric produced a heavy key from his belt. The lock turned with a solid, satisfying *clunk* that echoed up the stairwell.

The room beyond was a vault. It was circular, perhaps twenty paces across, with a domed ceiling lost in shadow. The walls were lined not with shelves, but with hundreds of small, identical iron doors, each about the size of a folio, each with its own lock. In the centre of the room stood a single, large table of polished ebony, and upon it, on a cushion of dark velvet, lay a fragment of vellum.

“The *planh*,” Alaric said. He did not gesture with drama. His mere presence beside the table was the gesture.

I approached. The fragment was smaller than I had imagined, no larger than my spread hand. The vellum was dark with age, the edges brittle and irregular, as if torn from a larger sheet. The script was a cramped, late Carolingian minuscule, the ink faded to a sepia brown. I did not need to touch it to feel its exhaustion. It had been wept over, chanted, folded into a dead man’s hands, buried in the dark, exhumed, studied, and now it lay here, under the cold eye of a lamp, a specimen.

“You may examine it,” Alaric said. “Your hands are clean.”

The permission felt like a violation. I took a breath, steadying myself, and leaned close. I let my eyes unfocus, not reading the words, but seeing the texture of the skin, the ghost of the hair follicles, the faint, uneven ruling lines. I inhaled. The scent was faint: old sorrow, salt, earth. And underneath, the cold, mineral smell of this room.

*Do not remember yet*, I warned myself. *Not here. Not in front of him. The first memory must be pure, must be for the song alone, not for this performance.*

I straightened. “It is… fragile.”

“All true things are,” Alaric replied. “Our task is to make them durable. You understand the work? You are not to copy it. You are to *know* it. To internalize its every nuance. Then, you will produce for us a new rendering. Not a facsimile, but a… a revitalization. A memory made perfect and permanent. We have other specialists working on the musical notation from contemporary treatises, on the historical context of the lament. You are the vessel for the text’s soul.”

I nodded. “And the tools?”

He led me to a side alcove I had not noticed. Here was a lectern, a pot of their approved ink (a uniform, glossy black), a selection of their pens, a stack of their prepared vellum—smooth, uniform, impersonal. It was a cell for remembering.

“You will work here, under the fragment’s influence, so to speak. You may spend the mornings in direct contemplation. The afternoons, you will draft. I will review your progress each evening. Do you have questions?”

“The singer,” I said, keeping my voice flat. “The one for whom it was written. Do we have a name?”

Alaric’s eyes sharpened, a needle-point of interest. “A pertinent question. We believe so. The fragment is part of a larger collection that references a *joglar* named Pèire de Valèria. A minor figure. He died of a fever in the spring of 1241, near Toulouse. This *planh* was likely composed for him by a rival, or perhaps a patron. The emotion, we theorize, is as much competitive as it is elegiac. A fascinating layer.”

He had given me a name. Pèire de Valèria. It was a gift and a trap. A name was a handle. It could make the memory too personal, too specific, could tie it to their historical framework and strangle its wilder truth. I had to hold it lightly.

“Thank you, Magister. That will be… helpful.”

“We provide the context. You provide the resonance.” He moved towards the door. “I will leave you to begin. Remember, Scribe Arren: precision is reverence. We are not artists. We are surgeons of the past.”

He left, and the iron-bound door closed behind him with a soft, final sigh.

Alone in the vault, the silence deepened. It was the silence of a deep well. I turned to the fragment. Now, I could remember.

I did not look at the words. I closed my eyes. I placed my clean, dry hands on the edge of the ebony table and let the cold of it seep into my palms. I listened past the silence, past the hum of my own blood in my ears.

*First, the air*, I thought. Not this air. The air of its making.

I sought the memory in the vellum itself. The sheep that bore this skin had grazed on hillsides where the wind carried the smell of the Pyrenees. The lime used to treat it had been slaked in local water. The scribe who wrote upon it had breathed air thick with woodsmoke and the damp of a stone chamber, had blinked in the light of a tallow dip that spat and guttered.

The memory did not come as a vision. It came as a pressure, a tuning of my inner ear. A flavour at the back of my mind—bitter, like willow bark.

Then, the words began to detach themselves from the physical fragment. They floated in the dark behind my eyes, not as ink, but as sound.

*“Aquel temps de dolor…”* That time of sorrow…

It was a man’s voice, rough with grief and cheap wine. Not a trained singer. A man’s voice breaking. He was not in a court. He was in a low-ceilinged room. The floor was packed earth. I could smell the wine, yes, and unwashed wool, and the greasy smoke of a dying fire.

The voice cracked on the melisma of *“dolor.”* It was not a beautiful crack. It was the sound of a heart-string snapping. The composer was not crafting a masterpiece; he was staunching a wound with sound. His rival, Pèire, was not a beloved friend. He was a thorn, a goad, a mirror. His death was an insult, a theft of future contests, future insults. The grief was real, but it was tangled with rage, with a furious, unacknowledged loneliness.

This was not the clean, sad emotion the Collegium would want. This was a messy, human snarl.

I opened my eyes. The fragment lay inert, a corpse on velvet. But the song was alive now, coiled inside me, a strange, hot, and uncomfortable thing. I had remembered it. Not their historical artifact, but the living moment of its birth—ugly, raw, and true.

My plan of transference now had a shape. This was what I had to give away. Not a polished lament, but this ragged cry of complicated loss. This would be the seed I would plant in the common soil.

The afternoons became a strange, double-edged labour. At the ebony table, I produced drafts for Alaric. I rendered the *planh* in my finest, most disciplined hand. I chose words of measured sorrow, of dignified loss. I created the artifact they desired: a clean, beautiful ghost.

But in my mind, I rehearsed the true song. I felt the crack in the voice, the heat of the petty resentment mixed with the chill of mortality. I memorized the true melody, which did not follow the neat modes of their treatises but wavered, descended into a growl, then leapt into a frail, almost mocking falsetto on the word *“ombra”*—shadow.

During my allotted hour of exercise in the walled garden of the Collegium—a place of gravel paths and medicinal herbs, all labelled—I began to look for my singer. The garden was sometimes used by others: junior conservators taking air, servants on errands. I saw a young man once, mending a sack by the gate. He had a broad, plain face and thick fingers, but he was humming to himself, a folk tune about a miller’s daughter. His voice was untrained, but it had a core of brass, a natural carry. I filed him away.

A week into my tenure, Alaric’s evening review took a new turn. He had been pleased with my drafts, his comments minor adjustments to spacing, to the shade of my ink. But this night, he set my vellum aside and steepled his fingers.

“Your work is exemplary, Scribe Arren. The discipline is evident. This pleases me. It also presents an opportunity.”

He unlocked one of the small iron doors in the circular wall. From it, he drew not a fragment, but a bound codex, small and thick. He laid it on the table with a soft thud.

“The *planh* is, in a way, a test. A gauge of your… capacity for resonant memory. You have passed. This,” he said, resting a possessive hand on the codex, “is the true work.”

“What is it?”

“It has no name. We call it the *Cantica Occulta*—The Hidden Songs. It is a collection. Fragments, whispers, half-remembered charms, verses from rites that left no other trace. Heretical hymns, some say. Pagan remnants. Others, the first, fumbling poetry of a faith not yet hardened into doctrine. They are dangerous memories. Not for their content, which is mostly nonsense, but for their form. They are infectious. They resist fixation. Scribes who work on them sometimes find their own hand altering, adopting the peculiar glyphs. They report dreams of the words, spoken in voices that are not their own.”
